<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Ulysses_Grant_sidebar" title="Template:Ulysses Grant sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>

<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Ulysses_Grant_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>

<p><b>Ulysses Simpson Grant</b> (born <b>Hiram Ulysses Grant</b>; April 27, 1822 – July 23, 1885) was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">18th President of the United States</a> (1869–77). As <a href="/wiki/Commanding_General_of_the_United_States_Army" title="Commanding General of the United States Army">Commanding General</a> (1864–69), Grant worked closely with President <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> to lead the <a href="/wiki/Union_Army" title="Union Army">Union Army</a> to victory over the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederacy</a> in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>. He implemented Congressional <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era" title="Reconstruction Era">Reconstruction</a>, often at odds with President <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a>. Twice elected president, Grant led the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republicans</a> in their effort to remove the vestiges of Confederate nationalism and slavery, protect <a href="/wiki/African_American" class="mw-redirect" title="African American">African American</a> citizenship, and support economic prosperity. His presidency has often been criticized for tolerating corruption and for the severe economic depression in his second term.</p>
<p>Grant graduated in 1843 from the United States Military Academy at <a href="/wiki/United_States_Military_Academy_at_West_Point" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Military Academy at West Point">West Point</a>, then served in the <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" title="Mexican–American War">Mexican–American War</a>. After the war he married <a href="/wiki/Julia_Boggs_Dent" class="mw-redirect" title="Julia Boggs Dent">Julia Boggs Dent</a> in 1848, their marriage producing four children. Grant initially retired from the Army in 1854. He struggled financially in civilian life. When the Civil War began in 1861, he rejoined the U.S. Army. In 1862, Grant took control of <a href="/wiki/Kentucky_in_the_American_Civil_War" title="Kentucky in the American Civil War">Kentucky</a> and most of <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_in_the_American_Civil_War" title="Tennessee in the American Civil War">Tennessee</a>, and led Union forces to victory in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Shiloh" title="Battle of Shiloh">Battle of Shiloh</a>, earning a reputation as an aggressive commander. He incorporated displaced African American slaves into the Union war effort. In July 1863, after a series of coordinated battles, Grant defeated Confederate armies and seized <a href="/wiki/Vicksburg_Campaign" title="Vicksburg Campaign">Vicksburg</a>, giving the Union control of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> and dividing the Confederacy in two. After his victories in the <a href="/wiki/Chattanooga_Campaign" title="Chattanooga Campaign">Chattanooga Campaign</a>, Lincoln promoted him to lieutenant general and Commanding General of the United States Army in March 1864. Grant confronted <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Lee" title="Robert E. Lee">Robert E. Lee</a> in a series of bloody battles, trapping Lee's army in their <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Petersburg" title="Siege of Petersburg">defense of Richmond</a>. Grant coordinated a series of devastating campaigns in other theaters, as well. In April 1865, Lee surrendered to Grant at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Appomattox_Court_House" title="Battle of Appomattox Court House">Appomattox</a>, effectively ending the war. Historians have hailed Grant's military genius, and <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_and_the_American_Civil_War" title="Ulysses S. Grant and the American Civil War">his strategies</a> are featured in military history textbooks, but a minority contend that he won by brute force rather than superior strategy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper2004271.E2.80.93282_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper2004271.E2.80.93282-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the Civil War, Grant led the army's supervision of Reconstruction in the former Confederate states. Elected president in 1868 and reelected in 1872, he stabilized the nation during that turbulent period, prosecuted the <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan" title="Ku Klux Klan">Ku Klux Klan</a>, and enforced civil rights and voting rights laws using the army and the newly created <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">Department of Justice</a>. He also used the army to build the Republican Party in the South. After the <a href="/wiki/Fourteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution#Participants_in_rebellion" title="Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">disenfranchisement</a> of some former Confederates, Republicans gained majorities, and African Americans were elected to Congress and high state offices. In his second term, the Republican coalitions in the South splintered and were defeated one by one as <a href="/wiki/Redeemers" title="Redeemers">redeemers</a> (conservative whites) regained control using coercion and violence. In May 1875, Grant authorized his Secretary of Treasury <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Bristow" title="Benjamin Bristow">Benjamin Bristow</a> to shut down and prosecute the corrupt <a href="/wiki/Whiskey_Ring" title="Whiskey Ring">Whiskey Ring</a>. Grant's Indian Peace Policy, incorporating Christian missionaries, initially reduced frontier violence, but it is best known for the <a href="/wiki/Great_Sioux_War_of_1876" title="Great Sioux War of 1876">Great Sioux War of 1876</a>. Grant allowed the destruction of bison on the Western Plains to keep Indians on their reservations. Grant responded to charges of corruption in executive offices more than any other 19th Century president. He appointed the first <a href="/wiki/United_States_Civil_Service_Commission" title="United States Civil Service Commission">Civil Service Commission</a> and signed legislation ending the corrupt moiety system.</p>
<p>In foreign policy, Grant sought to increase trade and influence while remaining at peace with the world. His administration successfully resolved the <a href="/wiki/Alabama_Claims" title="Alabama Claims"><i>Alabama</i> claims</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Washington_(1871)" title="Treaty of Washington (1871)">Treaty of Washington</a> with <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">Great Britain</a>, ending wartime tensions. Grant avoided war with Spain over the <a href="/wiki/Virginius_Affair" title="Virginius Affair"><i>Virginius</i> Affair</a>, but Congress rejected his attempted <a href="/wiki/Annexation_of_Santo_Domingo" title="Annexation of Santo Domingo">annexation of the Dominican Republic</a>. His administration implemented a <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a> and sought to strengthen the dollar. Corruption charges escalated during his second term, while his response to the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1873" title="Panic of 1873">Panic of 1873</a> proved ineffective nationally in halting the five-year industrial depression that produced high unemployment, low prices, low profits, and bankruptcies. Grant left office in 1877 and embarked on a two-year diplomatic world tour that captured the nation's attention.</p>
<p>In 1880, Grant was unsuccessful in obtaining the Republican presidential nomination for a third term. Facing severe investment reversals and dying of throat cancer, he wrote <a href="/wiki/Personal_Memoirs_of_Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Personal Memoirs of Ulysses S. Grant">his memoirs</a>, which proved to be a major literary work and financial success. His death in 1885 prompted an outpouring in support of national unity. <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant#Historical_reputation" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Historical assessment</a> of Grant's legacy has varied considerably over the years. Early historical evaluations were negative about Grant's presidency, often focusing on the corruption charges against his associates. This trend began to change in the later 20th century. Scholars in general <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">rank his presidency</a> below the average, but modern research, in part focusing on civil rights, evaluates his administration more positively.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Early_life_and_career_of_Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Early life and career of Ulysses S. Grant">Early life and career of Ulysses S. Grant</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GrantBirthplace.jpg" class="image"><img alt=" White clapboard house and outbuildings behind a white fence" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/GrantBirthplace.jpg/240px-GrantBirthplace.jpg" width="240" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/GrantBirthplace.jpg/360px-GrantBirthplace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/GrantBirthplace.jpg/480px-GrantBirthplace.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1728" data-file-height="1152" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GrantBirthplace.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Grant's birthplace, <a href="/wiki/Point_Pleasant,_Ohio" title="Point Pleasant, Ohio">Point Pleasant, Ohio</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hiram Ulysses Grant was born in <a href="/wiki/Point_Pleasant,_Ohio" title="Point Pleasant, Ohio">Point Pleasant, Ohio</a>, on April 27, 1822, to <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Root_Grant" title="Jesse Root Grant">Jesse Root Grant</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Tanner_(occupation)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tanner (occupation)">tanner</a> and merchant, and Hannah Grant (née Simpson).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely19816_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely19816-2">[2]</a></sup> His ancestors Matthew and Priscilla Grant arrived aboard the <i>Mary and John</i> at <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Bay_Colony" title="Massachusetts Bay Colony">Massachusetts Bay Colony</a> in 1630.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely19813_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely19813-3">[3]</a></sup> Grant's great-grandfather fought in the <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">French and Indian War</a>, and his grandfather Noah served in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bunker_Hill" title="Battle of Bunker Hill">Bunker Hill</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200121.E2.80.9322_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200121.E2.80.9322-4">[4]</a></sup> Afterward, Noah settled in <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> and married Rachel Kelley, the daughter of an <a href="/wiki/Irish_Americans" title="Irish Americans">Irish</a> pioneer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite20166_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite20166-5">[5]</a></sup> Their son Jesse, father of Ulysses S., was a <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig Party</a> supporter with abolitionist sentiments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHesseltine19574_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHesseltine19574-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Grant's father moved to <a href="/wiki/Point_Pleasant" class="mw-disambig" title="Point Pleasant">Point Pleasant</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a> in 1820 and found work as a foreman in a tannery.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite20168_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite20168-7">[7]</a></sup> Jesse soon met his future wife, Hannah, and the two were married on June 24, 1821.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite20168.E2.80.939_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite20168.E2.80.939-8">[8]</a></sup> Ten months later Hannah gave birth to their first child, a son.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely19815.E2.80.936White20169_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely19815.E2.80.936White20169-9">[9]</a></sup> At a family gathering several weeks later the son's name, Ulysses, was drawn from ballots placed in a hat. Wanting to honor his father-in-law, Jesse declared the boy to be Hiram Ulysses, though he would always refer to his common name as <i>Ulysses</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson20142.E2.80.933White20169.E2.80.9310_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson20142.E2.80.933White20169.E2.80.9310-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[a]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1823, the family moved to the village of <a href="/wiki/Georgetown,_Ohio" title="Georgetown, Ohio">Georgetown</a>, Ohio, where five more siblings were born: Simpson, Clara, Orvil, Jennie, and Mary.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongacre20066.E2.80.937_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongacre20066.E2.80.937-13">[12]</a></sup> At the age of five, young Grant began his formal education, starting at a subscription school and later was enrolled in two private schools.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981497White201616.2C_18_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981497White201616.2C_18-14">[13]</a></sup> In the winter of 1836–1837, Grant was a student at <a href="/wiki/John_Brett_Richeson_House#Maysville_Academy" title="John Brett Richeson House">Maysville Seminary</a>, and in the autumn of 1838 he attended <a href="/wiki/John_Rankin_(abolitionist)" title="John Rankin (abolitionist)">John Rankin</a>'s academy. In his youth, Grant developed an unusual ability to ride, work with, and control horses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely19818.2C_10.2C_140.E2.80.93141White201621_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely19818.2C_10.2C_140.E2.80.93141White201621-15">[14]</a></sup> Expressing a strong dislike for the tannery, Grant's father put Ulysses' ability with horses to use and gave him work driving wagon loads of supplies and transporting people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a8White201619_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a8White201619-16">[15]</a></sup> Unlike his siblings, Grant was not forced to attend church by his <a href="/wiki/Methodist" class="mw-redirect" title="Methodist">Methodist</a> parents;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongacre20066.E2.80.937Waugh200910_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongacre20066.E2.80.937Waugh200910-17">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[b]</a></sup> for the rest of his life, he prayed privately and never officially joined any denomination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson20142.E2.80.933Longacre20066.E2.80.937_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson20142.E2.80.933Longacre20066.E2.80.937-19">[17]</a></sup> To others, including late in life, his own son, Grant appeared to be an <a href="/wiki/Agnostic" class="mw-redirect" title="Agnostic">agnostic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaugh200910_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaugh200910-20">[18]</a></sup> He inherited some of Hannah's Methodist <a href="/wiki/Works_of_Piety" title="Works of Piety">piety</a> and quiet nature, while inheriting his father's <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig</a> political inclinations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite201620Simpson201420_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite201620Simpson201420-21">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_military_career_and_personal_life">Early military career and personal life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg" class="image"><img alt=" Engraving of young Grant in uniform" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg/170px-Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg" width="170" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg/255px-Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg/340px-Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1122" data-file-height="1586" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Second lieutenant Ulysses S. Grant in full dress uniform in 1843</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="West_Point_and_first_assignment">West Point and first assignment</span></h3>
<p>Grant's father wrote to Congressman <a href="/wiki/Thomas_L._Hamer" title="Thomas L. Hamer">Thomas L. Hamer</a> requesting that he nominate Ulysses to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Military_Academy" title="United States Military Academy">United States Military Academy</a> (USMA) at <a href="/wiki/West_Point,_New_York" title="West Point, New York">West Point, New York</a>. At first, Grant refused to attend, but his father convinced him to attend if accepted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200125_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200125-22">[20]</a></sup> When a <a href="/wiki/Cadet" title="Cadet">cadet</a> opening became available in March 1839, Hamer nominated the 16-year-old Grant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite201625_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite201625-23">[21]</a></sup> Hamer mistakenly wrote down "Ulysses S. Grant", which became his adopted name.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198112Smith200124.2C_83_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198112Smith200124.2C_83-24">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[c]</a></sup> Grant, despite his concerns about his academic ability, entered the academy on July 1, 1839, as a cadet and trained there for four years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite201625.2C_43Cullum1891170.E2.80.93171Simpson201410.E2.80.9311_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite201625.2C_43Cullum1891170.E2.80.93171Simpson201410.E2.80.9311-26">[23]</a></sup> His nickname became "Sam" among army colleagues since the initials "U.S." also stood for "<a href="/wiki/Uncle_Sam" title="Uncle Sam">Uncle Sam</a>". As he later recalled it, "a military life had no charms for me"; he was lax in his studies, but he achieved above-average grades in mathematics and geology.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198116Smith200126.E2.80.9328_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198116Smith200126.E2.80.9328-27">[24]</a></sup> Grant developed a reputation as a fearless and expert horseman and set an <a href="/wiki/Equestrianism" title="Equestrianism">equestrian</a> high-jump record that stood for almost 25 years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson_Quarterly_201511.E2.80.9312_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson_Quarterly_201511.E2.80.9312-28">[25]</a></sup> On Sundays, cadets were required to march in formation to and attend services at the academy's Episcopal church, a requirement that Grant disliked.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite201641_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite201641-29">[26]</a></sup> Seeking relief from military routine, he also studied under <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantic</a> artist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Walter_Weir" title="Robert Walter Weir">Robert Walter Weir</a> and produced nine surviving artworks. Grant spent more time reading books from the library than his academic texts, frequently reading works by <a href="/wiki/James_Fenimore_Cooper" title="James Fenimore Cooper">James Fenimore Cooper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Washington_Irving" title="Washington Irving">Washington Irving</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200126McFeely198116.E2.80.9317_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200126McFeely198116.E2.80.9317-30">[27]</a></sup> Within a year Grant reexamined his desire to leave the academy and later wrote, "on the whole I like this place very much".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson201413-14_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson201413-14-31">[28]</a></sup> Quiet by nature, Grant established a few intimate friends among fellow cadets, including <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Tracy_Dent" title="Frederick Tracy Dent">Frederick Tracy Dent</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Longstreet" title="James Longstreet">James Longstreet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rufus_Ingalls" title="Rufus Ingalls">Rufus Ingalls</a>. Grant was inspired both by his Commandant, Captain <a href="/wiki/Charles_Ferguson_Smith" title="Charles Ferguson Smith">C. F. Smith</a>, and General <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott" title="Winfield Scott">Winfield Scott</a>, who visited the academy to review the cadets. He later wrote concerning the military life, "there is much to dislike, but more to like".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a12.E2.80.9313_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a12.E2.80.9313-32">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>Grant graduated on July 1, 1843, ranked 21st in a class of 39, and was promoted to the rank brevet <a href="/wiki/Second_lieutenant" title="Second lieutenant">second lieutenant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite201638.2C_44Cullum1891170.E2.80.93171White2016_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite201638.2C_44Cullum1891170.E2.80.93171White2016-33">[30]</a></sup> Glad to leave the academy, he planned to resign his commission after his four-year term of duty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198116.2C_19_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198116.2C_19-34">[31]</a></sup> Despite his excellent horsemanship, he was not assigned to the cavalry (assignments were determined by class rank, not aptitude), but to the <a href="/wiki/4th_Infantry_Regiment_(United_States)" title="4th Infantry Regiment (United States)">4th Infantry Regiment</a>. He served as regimental <a href="/wiki/Quartermaster" title="Quartermaster">quartermaster</a>, managing supplies and equipment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200126.E2.80.9328Longacre200624_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200126.E2.80.9328Longacre200624-35">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Grant's first assignment after graduation took him to the <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Barracks" class="mw-redirect" title="Jefferson Barracks">Jefferson Barracks</a> near <a href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Louis, Missouri">St. Louis, Missouri</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200128.E2.80.9329Brands2012a15_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200128.E2.80.9329Brands2012a15-36">[33]</a></sup> Commanded by Colonel <a href="/wiki/Stephen_W._Kearny" title="Stephen W. Kearny">Stephen W. Kearny</a>, the barracks was the nation's largest military base in the west.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200128_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200128-37">[34]</a></sup> Grant was happy with his new commander, but looked forward to the end of his military service and a possible teaching career.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200130.E2.80.9333_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200130.E2.80.9333-38">[35]</a></sup> He spent some of his time in Missouri visiting Dent's family and became engaged to Dent's sister, <a href="/wiki/Julia_Grant" title="Julia Grant">Julia</a>, in 1844.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200130.E2.80.9333_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200130.E2.80.9333-38">[35]</a></sup> Four years later on August 22, 1848, they were married at Julia's home in <a href="/wiki/St._Louis" title="St. Louis">St. Louis</a>. Grant's abolitionist father Jesse, who strongly disapproved of the Dent's slave ownership, refused to attend the wedding, which took place without either of Grant's parents.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaugh200933Lasner2002King2012_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaugh200933Lasner2002King2012-39">[36]</a></sup> Grant's friend, and future Confederate General, James Longstreet, a cousin to Julia, was among the groomsmen.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200173.E2.80.9374Waugh200933_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200173.E2.80.9374Waugh200933-40">[37]</a></sup> At the end of the month, Grant brought Julia back to <a href="/wiki/Bethel,_Ohio" title="Bethel, Ohio">Bethel, Ohio</a>, to meet his family, who was warmly received.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198120.2C_26Bonekemper20118Simpson201449_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198120.2C_26Bonekemper20118Simpson201449-41">[38]</a></sup> They had four children: <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Dent_Grant" title="Frederick Dent Grant">Frederick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_Jr." title="Ulysses S. Grant Jr.">Ulysses Jr.</a> ("Buck"), <a href="/wiki/Nellie_Grant" title="Nellie Grant">Ellen</a> ("Nellie"), and <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Root_Grant_(politician)" title="Jesse Root Grant (politician)">Jesse</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200173_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200173-42">[39]</a></sup> After the wedding Grant obtained a two-month extension to his leave and returned to Saint Louis when he decided, with a wife to support, that he would remain in the army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson201449_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson201449-43">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mexican_American_War">Mexican American War</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Monterrey.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Battle_of_Monterrey.jpg/220px-Battle_of_Monterrey.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Battle_of_Monterrey.jpg/330px-Battle_of_Monterrey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Battle_of_Monterrey.jpg/440px-Battle_of_Monterrey.jpg 2x" data-file-width="598" data-file-height="390" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Monterrey.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Monterrey street fighting, 1846</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Amid rising tensions with Mexico following the United States' <a href="/wiki/Texas_annexation" title="Texas annexation">annexation of Texas</a>, President <a href="/wiki/John_Tyler" title="John Tyler">John Tyler</a> ordered Grant's unit to Louisiana as part of the <a href="/wiki/Army_of_Observation" class="mw-redirect" title="Army of Observation">Army of Observation</a> under <a href="/wiki/Major_general_(United_States)" title="Major general (United States)">Major General</a> <a href="/wiki/Zachary_Taylor" title="Zachary Taylor">Zachary Taylor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200135.E2.80.9337Brands2012a15.E2.80.9317_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200135.E2.80.9337Brands2012a15.E2.80.9317-44">[41]</a></sup> Unable to provoke Mexico into war at <a href="/wiki/Corpus_Christi,_Texas" title="Corpus Christi, Texas">Corpus Christi, Texas</a>, President <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">James K. Polk</a> ordered Taylor to march 150 miles south to the <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande" title="Rio Grande">Rio Grande</a>, a role Grant did not approve of. Marching back to <a href="/wiki/Fort_Texas" class="mw-redirect" title="Fort Texas">Fort Texas</a>, Grant experienced his first taste of combat on May 8, 1846, at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Palo_Alto" title="Battle of Palo Alto">Battle of Palo Alto</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198130.E2.80.9331_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198130.E2.80.9331-45">[42]</a></sup> With the <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" title="Mexican–American War">Mexican–American War</a> begun, Polk directed the army south in September 1846.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a23_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a23-46">[43]</a></sup> Although a quartermaster, Grant led a cavalry charge at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Resaca_de_la_Palma" title="Battle of Resaca de la Palma">Battle of Resaca de la Palma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198132.E2.80.9333_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198132.E2.80.9333-47">[44]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Monterrey" title="Battle of Monterrey">Monterrey</a> he demonstrated his equestrian ability, by volunteering to carry a dispatch through sniper-lined streets while hanging off the side of his horse, keeping the animal between him and the enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200156.E2.80.9357_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200156.E2.80.9357-48">[45]</a></sup> Polk, wary of Taylor's growing popularity, divided his forces, sending some troops (including Grant's unit) to form a new army under Major General <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott" title="Winfield Scott">Winfield Scott</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198134.E2.80.9335_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198134.E2.80.9335-49">[46]</a></sup> Traveling by sea, Scott's army landed at <a href="/wiki/Veracruz" title="Veracruz">Veracruz</a> and advanced toward <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a41.E2.80.9342_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a41.E2.80.9342-50">[47]</a></sup> The army met the Mexican forces at the battles of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Molino_del_Rey" title="Battle of Molino del Rey">Molino del Rey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chapultepec" title="Battle of Chapultepec">Chapultepec</a> outside Mexico City.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198136_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198136-51">[48]</a></sup> Grant yearned for a combat role and finally was allowed to take part in dangerous missions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198136Brands2012a43_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198136Brands2012a43-52">[49]</a></sup> At San Cosmé, men under Grant's direction dragged a disassembled <a href="/wiki/Howitzer" title="Howitzer">howitzer</a> into a church steeple, reassembled it, and bombarded nearby Mexican troops.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198136_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198136-51">[48]</a></sup> His bravery and initiative earned him <a href="/wiki/Brevet_(military)" title="Brevet (military)">brevet</a> promotions; he became a temporary captain while his permanent rank was lieutenant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson201444_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson201444-53">[50]</a></sup> On September 14, 1847, Scott's army marched into the city, and the Mexicans <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo" title="Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo">agreed to peace</a> soon afterward.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200167.E2.80.9368.2C_70.2C_73Brands2012a49_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200167.E2.80.9368.2C_70.2C_73Brands2012a49-54">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>During this war, Grant studied the tactics and strategies of Scott and others.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2014458_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2014458-55">[52]</a></sup> He later wrote that this is how he learned about military leadership, and in retrospect identified his leadership style with Taylor's. Even so, he believed that the Mexican War was wrong and that the territorial gains from the war were designed to expand slavery. "I was bitterly opposed to the measure," he said, "and to this day, regard the war, which resulted, as one of the most unjust ever waged by a stronger against a weaker nation." He opined that the Civil War was punishment inflicted on the nation for its aggression in Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198131.2C_37_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198131.2C_37-56">[53]</a></sup> During the war, Grant soon discovered his "moral courage" and began to consider a career in the army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite201675_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite201675-57">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pacific_west_and_resignation">Pacific west and resignation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Interior_of_a_Chinookan_plankhouse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Interior_of_a_Chinookan_plankhouse.jpg/220px-Interior_of_a_Chinookan_plankhouse.jpg" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Interior_of_a_Chinookan_plankhouse.jpg/330px-Interior_of_a_Chinookan_plankhouse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Interior_of_a_Chinookan_plankhouse.jpg/440px-Interior_of_a_Chinookan_plankhouse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1133" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Interior_of_a_Chinookan_plankhouse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Grant believed Pacific Northwest Indians were harmless. <a href="/wiki/Chinookan_peoples" title="Chinookan peoples">Chinook Indian Plankhouse</a> <i>1850s</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Grant's first post-war assignments took him and Julia to <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a> and then to <a href="/wiki/Sackets_Harbor,_New_York" title="Sackets Harbor, New York">Sackets Harbor, New York</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198144_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198144-58">[55]</a></sup> In 1852, Grant was ordered to the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest" title="Pacific Northwest">Pacific Northwest</a>, traveling <a href="/wiki/Maritime_history_of_California#To_California_via_Panama" title="Maritime history of California">the New York-Panama oceanic route</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200176_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200176-59">[56]</a></sup> Julia, who was eight months pregnant with Ulysses Jr., did not accompany him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200176.E2.80.9377_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200176.E2.80.9377-60">[57]</a></sup> In Panama, an outbreak of <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> among his fellow travelers caused 150 fatalities; Grant arranged makeshift transportation and hospital facilities to care for the sick.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198147_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198147-61">[58]</a></sup> In August, Grant arrived in San Francisco, and his next assignment sent him north to <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Barracks" title="Vancouver Barracks">Vancouver Barracks</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Territory" title="Oregon Territory">Oregon Territory</a> (subsequently <a href="/wiki/Washington_Territory" title="Washington Territory">Washington Territory</a> in March 1853).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200178_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200178-62">[59]</a></sup> To supplement a military salary which was inadequate to support his family, Grant tried and failed at several business ventures, confirming Jesse Grant's belief that his son had no head for business.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198148.E2.80.9349_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198148.E2.80.9349-63">[60]</a></sup> Grant assured Julia in a letter that local <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a> were harmless, while he developed an empathy for the plight of Indians from the "unjust treatment" by white men.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016487_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016487-64">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Promoted to <a href="/wiki/Captain_(U.S._Army)" class="mw-redirect" title="Captain (U.S. Army)">captain</a> on August 5, 1853, Grant was assigned to command Company F, 4th Infantry, at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Humboldt_State_Historic_Park" title="Fort Humboldt State Historic Park">Fort Humboldt</a> in California.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198152Cullum1891171_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198152Cullum1891171-65">[62]</a></sup> Grant arrived at the fort on January 5, 1854, and reported to its commander Lieutenant Colonel <a href="/wiki/Robert_C._Buchanan" title="Robert C. Buchanan">Robert C. Buchanan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016118_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016118-66">[63]</a></sup> Separated from his family with little to keep him busy at the isolated fortress, Grant began to drink.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016118.E2.80.93120_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016118.E2.80.93120-67">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[d]</a></sup> In letters to his wife Julia, Grant expressed his despair and considered leaving the fort, "willing or unwilling".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016118.E2.80.93120_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016118.E2.80.93120-67">[64]</a></sup> Army rumors hold that Buchanan was informed that Grant had become intoxicated off-duty while seated at the pay officer's table but because of his distinguished service in the Mexican War, Buchanan gave him the chance to resign instead of facing a court-martial. Grant did so, effective July 31, 1854, without explanation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECullum1891171_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECullum1891171-70">[66]</a></sup> Buchanan endorsed Grant's letter of resignation but did not submit any separate report.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200186.E2.80.9387White2016118.E2.80.93120_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200186.E2.80.9387White2016118.E2.80.93120-71">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[e]</a></sup> Not knowing how his wife would receive the news of his resignation and with no means of support, Grant returned to St. Louis and reunited with his family, uncertain about his future.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a77.E2.80.9378_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a77.E2.80.9378-75">[70]</a></sup> The War Department stated on his record, "Nothing stands against his good name."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongacre200655.E2.80.9358_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongacre200655.E2.80.9358-76">[71]</a></sup> After Grant's departure, rumors persisted in the regular army of his drinking.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198155_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198155-72">[68]</a></sup> Years later, he said, "the vice of intemperance (drunkenness) had not a little to do with my decision to resign."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200187.E2.80.9388Lewis1950328.E2.80.93332_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200187.E2.80.9388Lewis1950328.E2.80.93332-77">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Civilian_struggles_and_politics">Civilian struggles and politics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hardscrabble.tiff" class="image"><img alt="small log cabin" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Hardscrabble.tiff/lossy-page1-220px-Hardscrabble.tiff.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Hardscrabble.tiff/lossy-page1-330px-Hardscrabble.tiff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Hardscrabble.tiff/lossy-page1-440px-Hardscrabble.tiff.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3511" data-file-height="2497" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hardscrabble.tiff" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Hardscrabble", the home Grant built in Missouri for his family</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At age 32, with no civilian vocation, Grant struggled through seven financially lean years. His father initially offered him a place in the <a href="/wiki/Galena,_Illinois" title="Galena, Illinois">Galena, Illinois</a>, branch of the family's tannery and leather goods business, on condition that Julia and the children stay with her parents in Missouri or with the Grants in Kentucky. Ulysses and Julia opposed another separation and declined the offer. In 1855, Grant farmed on his brother-in-law's property near St. Louis, using slaves owned by Julia's father.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198158.E2.80.9360_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198158.E2.80.9360-78">[73]</a></sup> The farm was not successful and to earn money he sold firewood on St. Louis street corners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198161_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198161-79">[74]</a></sup> The next year, the Grants moved to land on Julia's father's farm, and built a home Grant called "<a href="/wiki/Grant%27s_Farm" title="Grant's Farm">Hardscrabble</a>". Julia disliked the rustic house, which she described as an "unattractive cabin".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198158.E2.80.9360_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198158.E2.80.9360-78">[73]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1857" title="Panic of 1857">Panic of 1857</a> devastated farmers, including Grant, who was forced to rent out Hardscrabble the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198162_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198162-80">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1857, Grant acquired a slave, a thirty-five-year-old man named William Jones, left behind by his father-in-law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200194.E2.80.9395_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200194.E2.80.9395-81">[76]</a></sup> Having met with no success farming, the Grants left the farm when their fourth and final child was born in 1858. The following year, Grant freed his slave, who was 35 years old and worth about $1,500, instead of selling him at a time when Grant desperately needed money.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200194.E2.80.9395McFeely198169_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200194.E2.80.9395McFeely198169-82">[77]</a></sup> The family then moved and took a small house in St. Louis where he worked with Julia's cousin Harry Boggs as a bill collector, again without success.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198164_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198164-83">[78]</a></sup> In 1860, Jesse offered him the job in Galena without conditions, and Grant accepted. The leather shop, "Grant &amp; Perkins", sold harnesses, saddles, and other leather goods, and purchased hides from farmers in the prosperous Galena area. Grant and family moved to a rental house that year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198165.E2.80.9366_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198165.E2.80.9366-84">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many considered Grant to be allied politically to his father-in-law, Frederick Dent, a prominent Missouri <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon19694.E2.80.935_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon19694.E2.80.935-85">[80]</a></sup> In the 1856 election, Grant cast his first presidential vote for the Democrat, <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">James Buchanan</a>, later saying he was really voting against Republican <a href="/wiki/John_C._Fr%C3%A9mont" title="John C. Frémont">John C. Frémont</a> over concern that his anti-slavery position would motivate southern states to secede.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198169.E2.80.9370_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198169.E2.80.9370-86">[81]</a></sup> In 1859, Grant's suspected Democratic leanings cost him an appointment to become county engineer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon19694.E2.80.935_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon19694.E2.80.935-85">[80]</a></sup> By the 1860 election, Grant was openly Democratic, favoring Democrat <a href="/wiki/Stephen_A._Douglas" title="Stephen A. Douglas">Stephen A. Douglas</a> over <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>, and Lincoln over the Southern Democrat, <a href="/wiki/John_C._Breckinridge" title="John C. Breckinridge">John C. Breckinridge</a>. Lacking the residency requirements in Illinois at the time, he could not vote.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198169.E2.80.9370Simon19694.E2.80.935_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198169.E2.80.9370Simon19694.E2.80.935-87">[82]</a></sup> After Lincoln was elected president, Southern states seceded from the Union forming a <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederacy</a>, seizing federal forts and institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016139.E2.80.93140Smith200197_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016139.E2.80.93140Smith200197-88">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_War">Civil War</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_and_the_American_Civil_War" title="Ulysses S. Grant and the American Civil War">Ulysses S. Grant and the American Civil War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USGrant01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/USGrant01.jpg/170px-USGrant01.jpg" width="170" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/USGrant01.jpg/255px-USGrant01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/USGrant01.jpg/340px-USGrant01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="383" data-file-height="568" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USGrant01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Brig. Gen. Grant in 1861</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On April 12, 1861, the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> began as Confederate troops <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Sumter" title="Battle of Fort Sumter">attacked Fort Sumter</a> in Charleston, South Carolina.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016140_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016140-89">[84]</a></sup> Two days later, President Lincoln called for 75,000 volunteers and a mass meeting was held in Galena to assess the crisis and encourage recruitment. As a military professional, Grant was asked to lead the ensuing recruitment effort.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016142.E2.80.93143_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016142.E2.80.93143-90">[85]</a></sup> The meeting was called to order by the captain of the local militia who then nominated Grant to chair the meeting.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016140Brands2012a121.E2.80.93122_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016140Brands2012a121.E2.80.93122-91">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Without any formal rank in the army, Grant quickly raised and recruited a company of volunteers, which he was given the captaincy, and accompanied them to <a href="/wiki/Springfield,_Illinois" title="Springfield, Illinois">Springfield</a>, the state capital.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a122.E2.80.93123_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a122.E2.80.93123-92">[87]</a></sup> Grant quickly perceived that the war would be fought for the most part by volunteers and not career soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198180_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198180-93">[88]</a></sup> Illinois Governor <a href="/wiki/Richard_Yates_(19th_century_politician)" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard Yates (19th century politician)">Richard Yates</a> offered Grant a militia commission to recruit and train volunteer units, which he accepted, but he still wanted a field command in the army. He made several efforts through his professional contacts, including <a href="/wiki/George_B._McClellan" title="George B. McClellan">Major General George B. McClellan</a>. McClellan refused to meet him, remembering Grant's earlier reputation for drinking while stationed in California.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood200543_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood200543-94">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Before the attack on Fort Sumter, Grant had not reacted strongly to Southern secession.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a121_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a121-95">[90]</a></sup> The news of the attack came as a shock in Galena, and Grant shared his neighbors' mounting concern about the onset of war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a121_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a121-95">[90]</a></sup> A speech by his father's attorney, <a href="/wiki/John_Aaron_Rawlins" title="John Aaron Rawlins">John Aaron Rawlins</a>, stirred Grant's patriotism and rekindled his military passion for the Union cause.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198173.E2.80.9376.2C_80Smith2001107.E2.80.93108Brands2012a121White2016142_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198173.E2.80.9376.2C_80Smith2001107.E2.80.93108Brands2012a121White2016142-96">[91]</a></sup> Rawlins later became Grant's <a href="/wiki/Aide-de-camp" title="Aide-de-camp">aide-de-camp</a> and close friend during the war. Grant recalled with satisfaction that after that first recruitment meeting in Galena, "I never went into our leather store again."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198173_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198173-97">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the aid of his advocate in Washington, Illinois congressman <a href="/wiki/Elihu_B._Washburne" title="Elihu B. Washburne">Elihu B. Washburne</a>, Grant was promoted to Colonel on June 14 and put in charge of disciplining the unruly <a href="/wiki/21st_Illinois_Volunteer_Infantry_Regiment" title="21st Illinois Volunteer Infantry Regiment">21st Illinois Volunteer Infantry Regiment</a>. To restore order, Grant had one recruit bound and gagged to a post for being drunk and disorderly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood200545.E2.80.9346_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood200545.E2.80.9346-98">[93]</a></sup> Transferred to northern Missouri, Grant was promoted by Lincoln to Brigadier General, backdated to May 17, 1861, again with Washburne's support.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001113_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001113-99">[94]</a></sup> Believing Grant was a general of "dogged persistence" and "iron will", <a href="/wiki/John_C._Fr%C3%A9mont" title="John C. Frémont">Major General John C. Frémont</a> assigned Grant command of troops near <a href="/wiki/Cairo,_Illinois" title="Cairo, Illinois">Cairo, Illinois</a>, by the end of August.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001117.E2.80.93118Catton196329_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001117.E2.80.93118Catton196329-100">[95]</a></sup> Under Frémont's authority, Grant advanced into <a href="/wiki/Paducah,_Kentucky" title="Paducah, Kentucky">Paducah</a> and took the town without a fight.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood200563_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood200563-101">[96]</a></sup> On November 1, Frémont ordered Grant to "<a href="/wiki/Demonstration_(military)" title="Demonstration (military)">make demonstrations</a>" against the Confederates on both sides of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi</a>, but prohibited him from attacking the enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016168_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016168-102">[97]</a></sup> The following day Lincoln relieved Frémont of command.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016168_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016168-102">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[f]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Belmont.2C_Forts_Henry_and_Donelson">Belmont, Forts Henry and Donelson</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Fort_Donelson.png" class="image"><img alt="Union soldiers charging into battle, some injured or dying" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Battle_of_Fort_Donelson.png/220px-Battle_of_Fort_Donelson.png" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Battle_of_Fort_Donelson.png/330px-Battle_of_Fort_Donelson.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Battle_of_Fort_Donelson.png/440px-Battle_of_Fort_Donelson.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="649" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Fort_Donelson.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Donelson" title="Battle of Fort Donelson">Battle of Fort Donelson</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On November 7, 1861, on his own initiative, Grant and his troops embarked south from Cairo on the Mississippi and attacked Confederate soldiers encamped in <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Belmont" title="Battle of Belmont">Belmont</a>, Missouri.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a151Flood200571Smith2001131White2016168.E2.80.93169_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a151Flood200571Smith2001131White2016168.E2.80.93169-104">[98]</a></sup> They took Camp Johnson, but the reinforced Confederates under Brigadier Generals <a href="/wiki/Frank_Cheatham" class="mw-redirect" title="Frank Cheatham">Frank Cheatham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gideon_J._Pillow" class="mw-redirect" title="Gideon J. Pillow">Gideon J. Pillow</a> forced a chaotic Union retreat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016172_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016172-105">[99]</a></sup> Grant and his troops fought their way back to their Union boats and escaped back to Cairo under Confederate fire.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016172.E2.80.93173_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016172.E2.80.93173-106">[100]</a></sup> A tactical defeat, the battle nonetheless gave Grant and his volunteers confidence and experience.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198192.E2.80.9394_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198192.E2.80.9394-107">[101]</a></sup> After Belmont, Grant asked his new commander <a href="/wiki/Henry_Halleck" title="Henry Halleck">Henry Halleck</a> for permission to move against <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Henry" title="Battle of Fort Henry">Fort Henry</a> in Tennessee, which would open the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_River" title="Tennessee River">Tennessee River</a> to Union gunboats; Halleck agreed on condition that the attack be conducted in close cooperation with navy <a href="/wiki/Flag_Officer#United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Flag Officer">Flag Officer</a> <a href="/wiki/Andrew_H._Foote" class="mw-redirect" title="Andrew H. Foote">Andrew H. Foote</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001138.E2.80.93142_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001138.E2.80.93142-108">[102]</a></sup> After Foote's gunboats had silenced most of the guns at the fort, Grant's troops moved in and captured Fort Henry on February 6, 1862.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001146_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001146-109">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Emboldened by Lincoln's call for a general advance of all Union forces, Grant ordered an immediate <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Donelson" title="Battle of Fort Donelson">assault</a> on nearby <a href="/wiki/Fort_Donelson" title="Fort Donelson">Fort Donelson</a>, which dominated the <a href="/wiki/Cumberland_River" title="Cumberland River">Cumberland River</a> (this time without Halleck's permission). On February 15, Grant and Foote met stiff resistance from Confederate forces under Pillow. After a day of battle, the Confederates submitted to Grant's demand for his "unconditional and immediate surrender."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001141.E2.80.93164Brands2012a164.E2.80.93165_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001141.E2.80.93164Brands2012a164.E2.80.93165-110">[104]</a></sup> Lincoln promoted Grant to major-general of volunteers while the Northern press treated Grant as a hero. Playing off his initials, they took to calling him "Unconditional Surrender Grant".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a164.E2.80.93165Smith2001125.E2.80.93134_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a164.E2.80.93165Smith2001125.E2.80.93134-111">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shiloh_and_aftermath">Shiloh and aftermath</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thure_de_Thulstrup_-_Battle_of_Shiloh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Union artillery firing through the woods; a messenger runs behind the line" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Thure_de_Thulstrup_-_Battle_of_Shiloh.jpg/220px-Thure_de_Thulstrup_-_Battle_of_Shiloh.jpg" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Thure_de_Thulstrup_-_Battle_of_Shiloh.jpg/330px-Thure_de_Thulstrup_-_Battle_of_Shiloh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Thure_de_Thulstrup_-_Battle_of_Shiloh.jpg/440px-Thure_de_Thulstrup_-_Battle_of_Shiloh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="9702" data-file-height="7018" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thure_de_Thulstrup_-_Battle_of_Shiloh.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Battle of Shiloh</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Shiloh" title="Battle of Shiloh">Battle of Shiloh</a></div>
<p>Encamped on the western bank of the Tennessee River, Grant's <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Tennessee" title="Army of the Tennessee">Army of the Tennessee</a>, now numbered about 45,000 troops and prepared to attack a Confederate army of roughly equal strength at <a href="/wiki/Corinth,_Mississippi" title="Corinth, Mississippi">Corinth, Mississippi</a>, a vital railroad junction. The Confederates, led by Generals <a href="/wiki/Albert_Sidney_Johnston" title="Albert Sidney Johnston">Albert Sidney Johnston</a> and <a href="/wiki/P.G.T._Beauregard" class="mw-redirect" title="P.G.T. Beauregard">P.G.T. Beauregard</a>, struck first on April 6, 1862, attacking five divisions of Grant's army bivouacked at <a href="/wiki/Pittsburg_Landing,_Tennessee" title="Pittsburg Landing, Tennessee">Pittsburg Landing</a>, not far from the Shiloh meetinghouse.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981111_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981111-112">[106]</a></sup> Grant's troops were not entrenched and were taken by surprise, falling back before the Confederate onslaught.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper201251.2C_94_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper201251.2C_94-113">[107]</a></sup> At day's end, the Confederates captured one Union division, but Grant's army was able to hold the Landing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper201251.2C_58.E2.80.9359.2C_63.E2.80.9364_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper201251.2C_58.E2.80.9359.2C_63.E2.80.9364-114">[108]</a></sup> The remaining Union army might have been destroyed, but the Confederates halted due to exhaustion, confusion, and a lack of reinforcements.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981114Flood2005109.2C_112_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981114Flood2005109.2C_112-115">[109]</a></sup> Grant, bolstered by 18,000 fresh troops from the divisions of Major Generals <a href="/wiki/Don_Carlos_Buell" title="Don Carlos Buell">Don Carlos Buell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lew_Wallace" title="Lew Wallace">Lew Wallace</a>, counterattacked at dawn the next day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper201263_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper201263-116">[110]</a></sup> The Northerners regained the field and forced the rebels to retreat back to Corinth.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper201259.2C_63.E2.80.9364_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper201259.2C_63.E2.80.9364-117">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Shiloh's aftermath, the Northern press criticized Grant for high casualties and for his alleged drunkenness during the battle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan20151109.E2.80.931119_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan20151109.E2.80.931119-118">[112]</a></sup> Shiloh was the costliest battle in American history to that point, with total casualties of about 23,800.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper201294_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper201294-119">[113]</a></sup> Halleck arrived at Pittsburg Landing on April 9, and removed Grant from field command, proceeding to <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Corinth" class="mw-redirect" title="First Battle of Corinth">capture Corinth</a>. Discouraged, Grant considered resigning his commission, but Brigadier General <a href="/wiki/William_Tecumseh_Sherman" title="William Tecumseh Sherman">William Tecumseh Sherman</a>, one of his division commanders, convinced him to stay.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a188.E2.80.93191_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a188.E2.80.93191-120">[114]</a></sup> Lincoln overruled Grant's critics, saying "I can't spare this man; he fights." Halleck reinstated Grant as field commander of the Army of the Tennessee on July 11.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongacre2006137_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongacre2006137-121">[115]</a></sup> On September 19, Grant's army defeated Confederates at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Iuka" title="Battle of Iuka">Battle of Iuka</a>, then successfully <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Corinth" title="Second Battle of Corinth">defended Corinth</a>, inflicting heavy casualties on the enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a211.E2.80.93212_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a211.E2.80.93212-122">[116]</a></sup> On October 25, Grant assumed command of the District of the Tennessee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBadeau1887126_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBadeau1887126-123">[117]</a></sup> In November, after Lincoln's preliminary <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">Emancipation Proclamation</a>, Grant ordered units under his command to incorporate contraband slaves into the Union war effort, giving them clothes, shelter, and wages for their services.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood2005133_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood2005133-124">[118]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vicksburg_campaign">Vicksburg campaign</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Vicksburg_Campaign" title="Vicksburg Campaign">Vicksburg Campaign</a> and <a href="/wiki/General_Order_No._11_(1862)" title="General Order No. 11 (1862)">General Order No. 11 (1862)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:VicksburgBlockade.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/VicksburgBlockade.jpg/220px-VicksburgBlockade.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/VicksburgBlockade.jpg/330px-VicksburgBlockade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/VicksburgBlockade.jpg/440px-VicksburgBlockade.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1300" data-file-height="924" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:VicksburgBlockade.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Grant's gamble: Porter's gunboats running the Confederate gauntlet at Vicksburg</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Confederate stronghold of <a href="/wiki/Vicksburg,_Mississippi" title="Vicksburg, Mississippi">Vicksburg</a> blocked the way for complete Union control of the Mississippi River and its capture was vital for the Union War effort.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016243_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016243-125">[119]</a></sup> Grant's Army held western Tennessee having almost 40,000 troops available to fight.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatton1960112_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatton1960112-126">[120]</a></sup> Grant was aggravated to learn that Lincoln authorized Major General <a href="/wiki/John_A._McClernand" class="mw-redirect" title="John A. McClernand">John A. McClernand</a> to raise a separate army for the purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a221.E2.80.93223_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a221.E2.80.93223-127">[121]</a></sup> Halleck ordered McClernand to <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Tennessee" title="Memphis, Tennessee">Memphis</a>, and placed him and his troops under Grant's authority.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood2005147.E2.80.93148White2016246_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood2005147.E2.80.93148White2016246-128">[122]</a></sup> After Grant's army captured <a href="/wiki/Holly_Springs,_Mississippi" title="Holly Springs, Mississippi">Holly Springs</a>, Grant planned to attack Vicksburg's front overland while Sherman would attack the fortress from the rear on the Mississippi River.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016248_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016248-129">[123]</a></sup> However, Confederate cavalry raids, on December 11 and 20, that broke Union communications and recaptured Holly Springs, prevented Grant's and Sherman's armies from connecting.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatton1960119.2C_291White2016248.E2.80.93249_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatton1960119.2C_291White2016248.E2.80.93249-130">[124]</a></sup> On December 29, a Confederate army led by <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_General_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lieutenant General (United States)">Lieutenant General</a> <a href="/wiki/John_C._Pemberton" title="John C. Pemberton">John C. Pemberton</a> repulsed Sherman's direct approach ascending the bluffs to Vicksburg at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chickasaw_Bayou" title="Battle of Chickasaw Bayou">Chickasaw Bayou</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a223.2C_225White2016250_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a223.2C_225White2016250-131">[125]</a></sup> McClernand reached Sherman's army, assumed command, and independently of Grant led a campaign that captured Confederate <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Arkansas_Post" title="Battle of Arkansas Post">Fort Hindman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper2012147.E2.80.93148_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper2012147.E2.80.93148-132">[126]</a></sup> During this time period Grant incorporated fleeing <a href="/wiki/African_American" class="mw-redirect" title="African American">African American</a> slaves into the Union Army giving them protection and paid employment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016246.E2.80.93247_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016246.E2.80.93247-133">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>Along with his military responsibilities in the months following Grant's return to command, he was concerned over an expanding illicit cotton trade in his district.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001-134">[128]</a></sup> He believed the trade undermined the Union war effort, funded the Confederacy, and prolonged the war, while Union soldiers died in the fields.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood2005143.E2.80.93144Sarna2012a37_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood2005143.E2.80.93144Sarna2012a37-135">[129]</a></sup> On December 17, he issued <a href="/wiki/General_Order_No._11_(1862)" title="General Order No. 11 (1862)">General Order No. 11</a>, expelling "Jews, as a class," from the district, saying that Jewish merchants were violating trade regulations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a218_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a218-136">[130]</a></sup> Writing in 2012, historian <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_D._Sarna" class="mw-redirect" title="Jonathan D. Sarna">Jonathan D. Sarna</a> said Grant "issued the most notorious anti-Jewish official order in American history."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESarna2012bSmith2001225_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESarna2012bSmith2001225-137">[131]</a></sup> Historians' opinions vary on Grant's motives for issuing the order.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001226.E2.80.93227Simpson2014164.E2.80.93165Brands2012a217.E2.80.93219_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001226.E2.80.93227Simpson2014164.E2.80.93165Brands2012a217.E2.80.93219-138">[132]</a></sup> Jewish leaders complained to Lincoln while the Northern press criticized Grant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a220Smith2001226.E2.80.93227_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a220Smith2001226.E2.80.93227-139">[133]</a></sup> Lincoln demanded the order be revoked and Grant rescinded it within three weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001226.E2.80.93227_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001226.E2.80.93227-140">[134]</a></sup> When interviewed years after the war, in response to accusations of his General Order being anti-Jewish, Grant explained: "During war times these nice distinctions were disregarded, we had no time to handle things with kid gloves."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsh2010368_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsh2010368-141">[135]</a></sup> Grant made amends with the Jewish community during his presidency, appointing them to various positions in his administration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESarna2012a89.2C_147_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESarna2012a89.2C_147-142">[136]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016494_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016494-143">[137]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Jackson_(cropped).tif" class="image"><img alt="Lines of soldiers fire at each other, with houses in the background" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Battle_of_Jackson_%28cropped%29.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Battle_of_Jackson_%28cropped%29.tif.jpg" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Battle_of_Jackson_%28cropped%29.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Battle_of_Jackson_%28cropped%29.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Battle_of_Jackson_%28cropped%29.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Battle_of_Jackson_%28cropped%29.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7070" data-file-height="4466" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Jackson_(cropped).tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jackson,_Mississippi" title="Battle of Jackson, Mississippi">Battle of Jackson</a>, fought on May 14, 1863, was part of the <a href="/wiki/Vicksburg_Campaign" title="Vicksburg Campaign">Vicksburg Campaign</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On January 29, 1863, Grant assumed personal overall command and attempted to advance his army through water-logged terrain to bypass Vicksburg's guns; these proved ineffective. In the process, however, Union soldiers gained experience for the lengthy campaign that lay ahead.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper2012148.E2.80.93149_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper2012148.E2.80.93149-144">[138]</a></sup> On April 16, 1863, Grant ordered Admiral <a href="/wiki/David_Dixon_Porter" title="David Dixon Porter">David Porter</a>'s gunboats south under fire from the Vicksburg batteries to meet up with his troops who had marched south down the west side of the Mississippi River.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a226.E2.80.93228_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a226.E2.80.93228-145">[139]</a></sup> Grant ordered diversionary battles, confusing Pemberton and allowing Grant's army to move east across the Mississippi, landing troops at <a href="/wiki/Bruinsburg,_Mississippi" title="Bruinsburg, Mississippi">Bruinsburg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood2005160_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood2005160-146">[140]</a></sup> Continuing eastward, Grant's army captured <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jackson,_Mississippi" title="Battle of Jackson, Mississippi">Jackson</a>, the state capital. Advancing his army to Vicksburg, Grant defeated Pemberton's army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Champion_Hill" title="Battle of Champion Hill">Battle of Champion Hill</a> on May 16, forcing Pemberton to retreat into Vicksburg.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood2005164.E2.80.93165_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood2005164.E2.80.93165-147">[141]</a></sup> After Grant's men assaulted the Vicksburg entrenchments twice, suffering severe losses, they settled in for <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Vicksburg" title="Siege of Vicksburg">a siege lasting seven weeks</a>. Pemberton surrendered Vicksburg to Grant on July 4, 1863.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981122.E2.80.93138Smith2001206.E2.80.93257_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981122.E2.80.93138Smith2001206.E2.80.93257-148">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>The fall of Vicksburg gave Union forces control of the Mississippi River and split the Confederacy in two. By that time, Grant's political sympathies fully coincided with the <a href="/wiki/Radical_Republicans" class="mw-redirect" title="Radical Republicans">Radical Republicans</a>' aggressive prosecution of the war and emancipation of the slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatton19688_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatton19688-149">[143]</a></sup> Although the success at Vicksburg was a morale boost for the Union war effort, Grant was criticized for his decisions and his alleged drunkenness.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981128.2C_135_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981128.2C_135-150">[144]</a></sup> The personal rivalry between McClernand and Grant continued after Vicksburg, until Grant removed McClernand from command when he contravened Grant by publishing an order without permission.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981136_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981136-151">[145]</a></sup> When <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a> <a href="/wiki/Edwin_M._Stanton" class="mw-redirect" title="Edwin M. Stanton">Edwin M. Stanton</a> suggested Grant be brought back east to run the <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Potomac" title="Army of the Potomac">Army of the Potomac</a>, Grant demurred, writing that he knew the geography and resources of the West better and he did not want to upset the chain of command in the East.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatton19687_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatton19687-152">[146]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chattanooga_and_promotion">Chattanooga and promotion</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Chattanooga_Campaign" title="Chattanooga Campaign">Chattanooga Campaign</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Missionary_Ridge_McCormick_Harvesting.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Confederate soldiers face Union troops running toward them" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Battle_of_Missionary_Ridge_McCormick_Harvesting.jpg/220px-Battle_of_Missionary_Ridge_McCormick_Harvesting.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Battle_of_Missionary_Ridge_McCormick_Harvesting.jpg/330px-Battle_of_Missionary_Ridge_McCormick_Harvesting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Battle_of_Missionary_Ridge_McCormick_Harvesting.jpg/440px-Battle_of_Missionary_Ridge_McCormick_Harvesting.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1353" data-file-height="965" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Missionary_Ridge_McCormick_Harvesting.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Union troops swarm <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Missionary_Ridge" title="Battle of Missionary Ridge">Missionary Ridge</a> and defeat Bragg's army.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Lincoln promoted Grant to major general in the regular army and subsequently assigned him command of the newly formed <a href="/wiki/Military_Division_of_the_Mississippi" title="Military Division of the Mississippi">Division of the Mississippi</a> on October 16, 1863, including the Armies of the <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Ohio" title="Army of the Ohio">Ohio</a>, Tennessee, and <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Cumberland" title="Army of the Cumberland">Cumberland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a265Cullum1891172NewellShrader2011328_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a265Cullum1891172NewellShrader2011328-153">[147]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chickamauga" title="Battle of Chickamauga">Battle of Chickamauga</a>, the Army of the Cumberland retreated into Chattanooga, where they were trapped.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood2005196_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood2005196-154">[148]</a></sup> When informed of the situation, Grant put Major General <a href="/wiki/George_H._Thomas" class="mw-redirect" title="George H. Thomas">George H. Thomas</a> in charge of the besieged army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood2005201_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood2005201-155">[149]</a></sup> Taking command, Grant arrived in Chattanooga by horseback, with plans to resupply the city and break the siege.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a267McFeely1981145_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a267McFeely1981145-156">[150]</a></sup> Lincoln also sent Major General <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Hooker" title="Joseph Hooker">Joseph Hooker</a> and two divisions of
