the Army of the Potomac to assist.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a267.E2.80.93268_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a267.E2.80.93268-157">[151]</a></sup> Union forces captured Brown's Ferry and opened a supply line to Bridgeport.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981147Smith2001267.E2.80.93268Catton196846.2C_54_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981147Smith2001267.E2.80.93268Catton196846.2C_54-158">[152]</a></sup> On November 23, 1863, Grant organized three armies to attack at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Missionary_Ridge" title="Battle of Missionary Ridge">Missionary Ridge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lookout_Mountain" title="Battle of Lookout Mountain">Lookout Mountain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood2005214.E2.80.93215_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood2005214.E2.80.93215-159">[153]</a></sup> Two days later in the early morning, Hooker's forces successfully took Lookout Mountain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood2005214.E2.80.93215_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood2005214.E2.80.93215-159">[153]</a></sup> Grant ordered Thomas and the Army of the Cumberland to advance when Sherman's army failed to take Missionary Ridge from the northeast.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood2005216_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood2005216-160">[154]</a></sup> The Army of the Cumberland, led by Major General <a href="/wiki/Philip_Sheridan" title="Philip Sheridan">Philip Sheridan</a> and Brigadier General <a href="/wiki/Thomas_J._Wood" title="Thomas J. Wood">Thomas J. Wood</a>, charged uphill and captured the Confederate entrenchments on top of the ridge, forcing the rebels into disorganized retreat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood2005217.E2.80.93218_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood2005217.E2.80.93218-161">[155]</a></sup> The decisive battle gave the Union control of Tennessee and opened <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>, the heartland of the Confederacy, to Union invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981148.E2.80.93150_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981148.E2.80.93150-162">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 2, 1864, Lincoln promoted Grant to lieutenant general, giving him command of all Union Armies, answering only to the President.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood2005232McFeely1981148Cullum1891172_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood2005232McFeely1981148Cullum1891172-163">[157]</a></sup> Grant assigned Sherman the Division of the Mississippi and traveled east to Washington, meeting with Lincoln to devise a strategy of <a href="/wiki/Total_war" title="Total war">total war</a> against the Confederacy. After settling Julia into a house in Georgetown, Grant established his headquarters with General <a href="/wiki/George_Meade" title="George Meade">George Meade</a>'s Army of the Potomac in Culpeper, Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981156_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981156-164">[158]</a></sup> He devised a strategy of coordinated Union offensives, attacking the rebel armies at the same time to keep the Confederates from shifting reinforcements within their interior lines. Sherman was to pursue <a href="/wiki/Joseph_E._Johnston" title="Joseph E. Johnston">Joseph E. Johnston</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Army_of_Tennessee" title="Army of Tennessee">Army of Tennessee</a>, while Meade would lead the Army of the Potomac, with Grant in camp, to attack <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Lee" title="Robert E. Lee">Robert E. Lee</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Army_of_Northern_Virginia" title="Army of Northern Virginia">Army of Northern Virginia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatton1960190.2C_193_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatton1960190.2C_193-165">[159]</a></sup> Major General <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Butler_(politician)" class="mw-redirect" title="Benjamin Butler (politician)">Benjamin Butler</a> was to advance towards Richmond from the south, up the <a href="/wiki/James_River" title="James River">James River</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981157_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981157-166">[160]</a></sup> If Lee was forced south as expected, Grant would join forces with Butler's armies and be fed supplies from the James. Major General <a href="/wiki/Franz_Sigel" title="Franz Sigel">Franz Sigel</a> was to capture the railroad line at <a href="/wiki/Lynchburg,_Virginia" title="Lynchburg, Virginia">Lynchburg</a>, move east, and attack from the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Ridge_Mountains" title="Blue Ridge Mountains">Blue Ridge Mountains</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981157.E2.80.93175Smith2001313.E2.80.93339.2C_343.E2.80.93368_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981157.E2.80.93175Smith2001313.E2.80.93339.2C_343.E2.80.93368-167">[161]</a></sup> Grant knew that Lee had limited manpower and that a war of attrition fought on a battlefield without entrenchments would lead to Lee's defeat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981162.E2.80.93163_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981162.E2.80.93163-168">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>Grant was now riding a rising tide of popularity, and there was talk that a Union victory early in the year could lead to his candidacy for the presidency. He was aware of the rumors, but had ruled out a political candidacy; the possibility would soon vanish with delays on the battlefield.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981162.E2.80.93163_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981162.E2.80.93163-168">[162]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overland_Campaign_and_Union_victory">Overland Campaign and Union victory</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Overland_Campaign" title="Overland Campaign">Overland Campaign</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_crop_of_Cold_Harbor_photo.png" class="image"><img alt="Photograph of Grant in uniform leaning on a post in front of a tent" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Grant_crop_of_Cold_Harbor_photo.png/250px-Grant_crop_of_Cold_Harbor_photo.png" width="250" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Grant_crop_of_Cold_Harbor_photo.png/375px-Grant_crop_of_Cold_Harbor_photo.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Grant_crop_of_Cold_Harbor_photo.png/500px-Grant_crop_of_Cold_Harbor_photo.png 2x" data-file-width="1828" data-file-height="1704" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_crop_of_Cold_Harbor_photo.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Commanding General Grant at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cold_Harbor" title="Battle of Cold Harbor">Battle of Cold Harbor</a> in 1864</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sigel's and Butler's efforts sputtered, and Grant was left alone to fight Lee in a series of bloody battles known as the <a href="/wiki/Overland_Campaign" title="Overland Campaign">Overland Campaign</a>. Grant crossed the <a href="/wiki/Rapidan_River" title="Rapidan River">Rapidan River</a> on May 4, 1864, and attacked Lee in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Wilderness" title="Battle of the Wilderness">Battle of the Wilderness</a>, a hard-fought three-day battle with many casualties. Rather than retreat as his predecessors had done, Grant flanked Lee's army to the southeast and attempted to wedge his forces between Lee and Richmond at <a href="/wiki/Spotsylvania_Court_House,_Virginia" class="mw-redirect" title="Spotsylvania Court House, Virginia">Spotsylvania Court House</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981165_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981165-169">[163]</a></sup> Lee's army got to <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Spotsylvania_Court_House" title="Battle of Spotsylvania Court House">Spotsylvania</a> first, and a costly battle ensued, lasting thirteen days. During the battle, Grant attempted to break through Lee's line of defense, resulting in one of the bloodiest assaults of the Civil War, known as the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Bloody_Angle" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of the Bloody Angle">Battle of the Bloody Angle</a>. Unable to break Lee's defenses, Grant again flanked the Confederate army to the southeast, meeting at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_North_Anna" title="Battle of North Anna">North Anna</a>, where a battle lasted three days.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981169_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981169-170">[164]</a></sup> The Confederates had the defensive advantage, and Grant maneuvered his army to <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cold_Harbor" title="Battle of Cold Harbor">Cold Harbor</a>, a vital railroad hub that linked to Richmond, but Lee's men were again able to entrench against the Union assault. During the third day of the thirteen-day battle, Grant led a costly assault on Lee's trenches. As casualty reports became known in the North, heavy criticism fell on Grant, who was castigated as "the Butcher" by the Northern press after taking 52,788 casualties in the thirty days since crossing the Rapidan; Lee's army suffered 32,907 casualties, but he was less able to replace them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper201141.E2.80.9342_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper201141.E2.80.9342-171">[165]</a></sup> The costly Union assault at Cold Harbor was the second of two battles in the war that Grant later said he regretted (the other being his initial assault on the fortifications around Vicksburg). Undetected by Lee, Grant pulled out of Cold Harbor and moved his army south of the James River, freed Butler from <a href="/wiki/Bermuda_Hundred_Campaign" title="Bermuda Hundred Campaign">the Bermuda Hundred</a> (where the Rebels had surrounded his army), and advanced toward Petersburg, Richmond's central railroad hub.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981157.E2.80.93175Smith2001313.E2.80.9339.2C_343.E2.80.9368_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981157.E2.80.93175Smith2001313.E2.80.9339.2C_343.E2.80.9368-172">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>After crossing the James, the Army of the Potomac <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Petersburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Petersburg">arrived at Petersburg</a>. Beauregard defended the city, and Lee's veteran reinforcements soon arrived. The result was a nine-month siege of Petersburg, stalling the advance. Northern resentment grew as the war dragged on, but an indirect benefit of the Petersburg siege was that Lee was forced to entrench and defend Richmond, and was unable to reinforce the Army of the Tennessee. Sheridan was assigned command of the <a href="/wiki/Union_Army_of_the_Shenandoah" title="Union Army of the Shenandoah">Union Army of the Shenandoah</a> and Grant directed him to "follow the enemy to their death". Lee had sent General <a href="/wiki/Jubal_Early" title="Jubal Early">Jubal Early</a> up the <a href="/wiki/Shenandoah_Valley" title="Shenandoah Valley">Shenandoah Valley</a> to attack the federal capital and draw troops away from the Army of the Potomac, but Sheridan defeated Early, ensuring Washington's safety. Grant then ordered Sheridan's cavalry to destroy vital Confederate supplies in the Shenandoah Valley. When Sheridan reported suffering attacks by irregular Confederate cavalry under <a href="/wiki/John_S._Mosby" title="John S. Mosby">John S. Mosby</a>, Grant recommended rounding up their families for imprisonment as hostages at Fort McHenry.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981178.E2.80.93186_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981178.E2.80.93186-173">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>At Petersburg, Grant approved a plan to <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Crater" title="Battle of the Crater">blow up</a> part of the enemy trenches from an underground tunnel. The explosion created a crater, into which poorly led Union troops poured. Recovering from the surprise, Confederates surrounded the crater and easily picked off Union troops within it. The Union's 3500 casualties outnumbered the Confederates' by three-to-one; although the plan could have been successful if implemented correctly, Grant admitted the tactic had been a "stupendous failure".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981179Smith2001369.E2.80.93395Catton1968308.E2.80.93309_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981179Smith2001369.E2.80.93395Catton1968308.E2.80.93309-174">[168]</a></sup> Rather than fight Lee in a full frontal attack as he had done at Cold Harbor, Grant continued to extend Lee's defenses south and west of Petersburg to capture vital railroad links.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatton1968309_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatton1968309-175">[169]</a></sup> As Grant continued to push the Union advance westward, Lee's lines became overstretched and undermanned. After the Federal army rebuilt the <a href="/wiki/City_Point_Railroad" title="City Point Railroad">City Point Railroad</a>, Grant was able to use mortars to attack Lee's entrenchments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatton1968294_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatton1968294-176">[170]</a></sup> On September 2, Sherman captured <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Atlanta" title="Battle of Atlanta">Atlanta</a> while Confederate forces retreated, ensuring Lincoln's reelection in November.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatton1960223.2C_228_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatton1960223.2C_228-177">[171]</a></sup> Sherman convinced Grant and Lincoln to send his army to <a href="/wiki/Sherman%27s_March_to_the_Sea" title="Sherman's March to the Sea">march on Savannah</a> devastating the Confederate heartland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatton1960235_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatton1960235-178">[172]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Peacemakers_1868.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Painting of four men conferring in a ship's cabin, entitled &quot;The Peacemakers&quot;." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/The_Peacemakers_1868.jpg/220px-The_Peacemakers_1868.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/The_Peacemakers_1868.jpg/330px-The_Peacemakers_1868.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/The_Peacemakers_1868.jpg/440px-The_Peacemakers_1868.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2254" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Peacemakers_1868.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Grant (center left) next to Lincoln with General <a href="/wiki/William_Tecumseh_Sherman" title="William Tecumseh Sherman">Sherman</a> (far left) and Admiral <a href="/wiki/David_Dixon_Porter" title="David Dixon Porter">Porter</a> (right) — <i><a href="/wiki/The_Peacemakers" title="The Peacemakers">The Peacemakers</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In late March 1865, Grant's forces finally took Petersburg, then captured Richmond that April. Grant, Sherman, Admiral Porter, and Lincoln held a conference on the <i>River Queen</i> to discuss the surrender of Confederate armies and <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era" title="Reconstruction Era">Reconstruction</a> of the South.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981213.E2.80.93214_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981213.E2.80.93214-179">[173]</a></sup> Lee's troops began deserting in large numbers; disease and lack of supplies also diminished the remaining Confederates. Lee attempted to link up with the remnants of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_E._Johnston" title="Joseph E. Johnston">Joseph E. Johnston</a>'s defeated army, but Sheridan's cavalry stopped the two armies from converging, cutting them off from their supply trains. Lee and his army surrendered to Grant at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Appomattox_Court_House" title="Battle of Appomattox Court House">Appomattox Court House</a> on April 9, 1865. Going beyond his military authority, Grant, in effect, gave Lee and his men amnesty; Confederates surrendered their weapons and were allowed to return to their homes, on the condition that they would not take up arms against the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoethals201592_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoethals201592-180">[174]</a></sup> On April 26, Johnson's army surrendered to Sherman under the same terms Grant offered to Lee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatton1960304_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatton1960304-181">[175]</a></sup> On May 26, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Kirby_Smith" title="Edmund Kirby Smith">Kirby Smith's</a> western army surrendered and the Civil War was over, ending in Union victory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981212.2C_219.E2.80.93220Catton1960304_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981212.2C_219.E2.80.93220Catton1960304-182">[176]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lincoln.27s_assassination">Lincoln's assassination</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Abraham_Lincoln" title="Assassination of Abraham Lincoln">Assassination of Abraham Lincoln</a></div>
<p>On April 14, five days after Grant's victory at Appomattox, he attended a cabinet meeting in Washington. Lincoln invited him and his wife to <a href="/wiki/Ford%27s_Theater" class="mw-redirect" title="Ford's Theater">Ford's Theater</a>, but they declined as they had plans to travel to Philadelphia. In a conspiracy that targeted several government leaders, Lincoln was fatally shot by <a href="/wiki/John_Wilkes_Booth" title="John Wilkes Booth">John Wilkes Booth</a> at the theater, and died the next morning.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981224_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981224-183">[177]</a></sup> Many, including Grant himself, thought that he had been a target in the plot.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a375.E2.80.93376_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a375.E2.80.93376-184">[178]</a></sup> Stanton notified him of the President's death and summoned him back to Washington. Attending Lincoln's funeral on April 19, Grant stood alone and wept openly; he later said Lincoln was "the greatest man I have ever known."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001409.E2.80.93412_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001409.E2.80.93412-185">[179]</a></sup> Regarding the new President, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a>, Grant told Julia that he dreaded the change in administrations; he judged Johnson's attitude toward white southerners as one that would "make them unwilling citizens", and initially thought that with President Johnson, "Reconstruction has been set back no telling how far."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981227.E2.80.93229_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981227.E2.80.93229-186">[180]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commanding_General">Commanding General</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_as_commanding_general,_1865%E2%80%931869" title="Ulysses S. Grant as commanding general, 1865–1869">Ulysses S. Grant as commanding general, 1865–1869</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beginning_Reconstruction">Beginning Reconstruction</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ole_Peter_Hansen_Balling_-_Portrait_of_Ulysses_S._Grant_(1865)_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ole_Peter_Hansen_Balling_-_Portrait_of_Ulysses_S._Grant_%281865%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/180px-Ole_Peter_Hansen_Balling_-_Portrait_of_Ulysses_S._Grant_%281865%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="180" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ole_Peter_Hansen_Balling_-_Portrait_of_Ulysses_S._Grant_%281865%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/270px-Ole_Peter_Hansen_Balling_-_Portrait_of_Ulysses_S._Grant_%281865%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ole_Peter_Hansen_Balling_-_Portrait_of_Ulysses_S._Grant_%281865%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/360px-Ole_Peter_Hansen_Balling_-_Portrait_of_Ulysses_S._Grant_%281865%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6804" data-file-height="8560" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ole_Peter_Hansen_Balling_-_Portrait_of_Ulysses_S._Grant_(1865)_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist: <a href="/wiki/Ole_Peter_Hansen_Balling" title="Ole Peter Hansen Balling">Ole Peter Hansen Balling</a>, Sitter: Ulysses Simpson Grant, Date: 1865</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the war's end, Grant remained commander of the army, with duties that included enforcement of Reconstruction in the former Confederate states and supervision of Indian wars on the western Plains.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a410.E2.80.93411_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a410.E2.80.93411-187">[181]</a></sup> Grant secured a house for his family in Georgetown Heights in 1865, but instructed Elihu Washburne that for political purposes his legal residence remained in Galena, Illinois.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981232.E2.80.93233_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981232.E2.80.93233-188">[182]</a></sup> That same year, Grant spoke at <a href="/wiki/Cooper_Union" title="Cooper Union">Cooper Union</a> in New York, where the <i>New York Times</i> reported that "...&#160;the entranced and bewildered multitude trembled with extraordinary delight." Further travels that summer took the Grants to <a href="/wiki/Albany,_New_York" title="Albany, New York">Albany, New York</a>, back to Galena, and throughout Illinois and Ohio, with enthusiastic receptions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981234_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981234-189">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>In November 1865, Johnson sent Grant on a fact-finding mission to the South. Grant recommended continuation of a reformed <a href="/wiki/Freedmen%27s_Bureau" title="Freedmen's Bureau">Freedmen's Bureau</a>, which Johnson opposed, but advised against the use of black troops in garrisons, which he believed encouraged an alternative to farm labor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a390Smith2001420McFeely1981238.E2.80.93241_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a390Smith2001420McFeely1981238.E2.80.93241-190">[184]</a></sup> Grant did not believe the people of the devastated South were ready for civilian self-rule, and that both whites and blacks in the South required protection by the federal government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a390_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a390-191">[185]</a></sup> He also warned of threats by disaffected poor people, black and white, and recommended that local decision-making be entrusted only to "thinking men of the South" (i.e., white men of property).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981238.2C_240_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981238.2C_240-192">[186]</a></sup> In this respect, Grant's opinion on Reconstruction aligned with Johnson's policy of pardoning established southern leaders and restoring them to their positions of power.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981240Smith2001420_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981240Smith2001420-193">[187]</a></sup> He joined Johnson in arguing that Congress should allow representatives from the South to take their seats.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981240.E2.80.93241_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981240.E2.80.93241-194">[188]</a></sup> On July 25, 1866, Congress promoted Grant to the newly created rank of <a href="/wiki/General_of_the_Army_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="General of the Army of the United States">General of the Army of the United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001434n_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001434n-195">[189]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breach_with_Johnson">Breach with Johnson</span></h3>
<p>Johnson favored a lenient approach to Reconstruction, calling for an immediate return of the former Confederate states into the Union without any guarantee of <a href="/wiki/African_American" class="mw-redirect" title="African American">African American</a> civil rights.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a397.E2.80.93398_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a397.E2.80.93398-196">[190]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Radical_Republican" title="Radical Republican">Radical Republican</a>-controlled Congress opposed the idea and refused to admit Congressmen from the former Confederate states.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a392.2C_396_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a392.2C_396-197">[191]</a></sup> Over Johnson's vetoes, Congress renewed the Freedmen's Bureau and passed the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1866" title="Civil Rights Act of 1866">Civil Rights Act of 1866</a>. During the congressional election campaign later that year, Johnson took his case to the people in his "<a href="/wiki/Swing_Around_the_Circle" title="Swing Around the Circle">Swing Around the Circle</a>" speaking tour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a396_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a396-198">[192]</a></sup> Johnson pressured Grant, by then the most popular man in the country, to go on the tour; Grant, wishing to appear loyal, agreed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001369.E2.80.93397Brands2012a389_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001369.E2.80.93397Brands2012a389-199">[193]</a></sup> Grant believed that Johnson was purposefully agitating conservative opinion to defy Congressional Reconstruction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a397.E2.80.93398_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a397.E2.80.93398-196">[190]</a></sup> Finding himself increasingly at odds with Johnson, Grant believed Johnson's speeches were a "national disgrace".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a397.E2.80.93398_196-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a397.E2.80.93398-196">[190]</a></sup> Publicly, Grant attempted to appear loyal to the President while not alienating Republican legislators essential to his future political career. Concerned that Johnson's differences with Congress would cause renewed insurrection, he ordered Southern arsenals to ship arms north to prevent their capture by Southern state governments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a398.E2.80.93399_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a398.E2.80.93399-200">[194]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rejecting Johnson's vision for quick reconciliation with former Confederates, Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Acts" title="Reconstruction Acts">Reconstruction Acts</a>, which divided the southern states into five military districts to protect the freedman's constitutional and congressional rights. Military governors were to lead transitional state governments in each district. Grant, who was to select the generals from a group designated by Johnson, preferred Congress's plan for enforcement of Reconstruction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001432.E2.80.93433_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001432.E2.80.93433-201">[195]</a></sup> Grant hoped that Reconstruction Acts would help pacify the South.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001434.E2.80.93435_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001434.E2.80.93435-202">[196]</a></sup> By complying with the Acts and instructing his subordinates to do likewise, Grant further alienated Johnson. When Sheridan removed public officials in Louisiana who impeded Reconstruction, Johnson was displeased and sought Sheridan's removal; Grant recommended a rebuke, but not a dismissal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981259.E2.80.93261Smith2001436.E2.80.93439_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981259.E2.80.93261Smith2001436.E2.80.93439-203">[197]</a></sup> Throughout Reconstruction, Grant and the military protected the rights of <a href="/wiki/List_of_African-American_officeholders_during_the_Reconstruction" class="mw-redirect" title="List of African-American officeholders during the Reconstruction">African Americans elected to political office</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001421.2C_433_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001421.2C_433-204">[198]</a></sup> On July 19, 1867, Congress, again over Johnson's veto, passed a measure giving Grant oversight in enforcing congressional Reconstruction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001438_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001438-205">[199]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Johnson.27s_impeachment">Johnson's impeachment</span></h3>
<p>Johnson wished to replace Secretary of War Stanton, a Lincoln appointee who sympathized with Congressional Reconstruction. To keep Grant under control as a potential political rival, Johnson asked him to take the post. Grant recommended against the move, in light of the <a href="/wiki/Tenure_of_Office_Act_(1867)" title="Tenure of Office Act (1867)">Tenure of Office Act</a>, which required Senate approval for cabinet removals. Johnson believed the Act did not apply to officers appointed by the previous president and forced the issue by making Grant an interim appointee on August 12, 1867, during a Senate recess.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECullum1891172_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECullum1891172-206">[200]</a></sup> Grant agreed to accept the post temporarily, and Stanton vacated the office until the Senate reconvened.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981262.E2.80.93264_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981262.E2.80.93264-207">[201]</a></sup></p>
<p>When the Senate reinstated Stanton, Johnson told Grant to refuse to surrender the office and let the courts resolve the matter. Grant told Johnson in private that violating the Tenure of Office Act was a federal offense, which could result in a fine or imprisonment. Believing he had no other legal alternatives, Grant returned the office to Stanton on January 14, 1868.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECullum1891172_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECullum1891172-206">[200]</a></sup> This incurred Johnson's wrath; at a cabinet meeting immediately afterward, Johnson accused Grant of breaking his promise to remain Secretary of War. Grant disputed that he had ever made such a promise although cabinet members later testified he had done so.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001448.E2.80.93451_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001448.E2.80.93451-208">[202]</a></sup> Newspapers friendly to Johnson published a series of articles to discredit Grant over returning the War Department to Stanton, stating that Grant had been deceptive in the matter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001448.E2.80.93451_208-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001448.E2.80.93451-208">[202]</a></sup> This public insult infuriated Grant, and he defended himself in an angry letter to Johnson, after which the two men were confirmed foes. When Grant's statement became public, it increased his popularity among Radical Republicans and he emerged from the controversy unscathed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001448.E2.80.93451_208-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001448.E2.80.93451-208">[202]</a></sup> Although Grant favored Johnson's impeachment, he took no active role in the <a href="/wiki/Impeachment_of_Andrew_Johnson" title="Impeachment of Andrew Johnson">impeachment proceedings</a>, which were fueled in part by Johnson's removal of Stanton. Johnson barely survived, and none of the other Republican leaders directly involved benefited politically in their unsuccessful attempt to remove the president.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981275_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981275-209">[203]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1868">Election of 1868</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Inauguration_of_Grant_-_NARA_-_530394.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph of crowd in front of Capitol building decorated with patriotic bunting" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Inauguration_of_Grant_-_NARA_-_530394.jpg/220px-Inauguration_of_Grant_-_NARA_-_530394.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Inauguration_of_Grant_-_NARA_-_530394.jpg/330px-Inauguration_of_Grant_-_NARA_-_530394.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Inauguration_of_Grant_-_NARA_-_530394.jpg/440px-Inauguration_of_Grant_-_NARA_-_530394.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2745" data-file-height="1947" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Inauguration_of_Grant_-_NARA_-_530394.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
First inauguration of Ulysses S. Grant on the steps of the Capitol on March 4, 1869</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1868" title="United States presidential election, 1868">United States presidential election, 1868</a></div>
<p>Grant entered the 1868 campaign season with increased popularity among the Radical Republicans following his abandonment of Johnson over the Secretary of War dispute. The Republicans chose Grant as their presidential candidate on the first ballot at the <a href="/wiki/1868_Republican_National_Convention" title="1868 Republican National Convention">1868 Republican National Convention</a> in Chicago.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[g]</a></sup> In his letter of acceptance, Grant concluded with "Let us have peace", which became his campaign slogan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981264.E2.80.93267_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981264.E2.80.93267-212">[205]</a></sup> For vice president, the delegates nominated House Speaker <a href="/wiki/Schuyler_Colfax" title="Schuyler Colfax">Schuyler Colfax</a>. Grant's 1862 <a href="/wiki/General_Order_No._11_(1862)" title="General Order No. 11 (1862)">General Order No. 11</a> became an issue during the <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_1868" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. presidential election, 1868">presidential campaign</a>; he sought to distance himself from the order, saying "I have no prejudice against sect or race, but want each individual to be judged by his own merit."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001459.E2.80.93460_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001459.E2.80.93460-213">[206]</a></sup> As President, Grant would atone for 1862's expulsion of the Jews. Historian <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Sarna" title="Jonathan Sarna">Jonathan Sarna</a> argues that Grant became one of the greatest friends of Jews in American history, meeting with them often and appointing them to high office. He was the first president to condemn atrocities against Jews in Europe, thus putting human rights on the American diplomatic agenda.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESarna2012axi.2C_88.E2.80.9390.2C_101.E2.80.93103_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESarna2012axi.2C_88.E2.80.9390.2C_101.E2.80.93103-214">[207]</a></sup> As was expected at the time, Grant returned to <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_Home" title="Ulysses S. Grant Home">his home</a> state<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[h]</a></sup> as well as vacationing in the West with his two closest friends, Sherman and Sheridan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016463_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016463-218">[210]</a></sup> He left the active campaigning and speaking on his behalf to his campaign manager, <a href="/wiki/William_E._Chandler" title="William E. Chandler">William E. Chandler</a>, and others.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981282.E2.80.93284_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981282.E2.80.93284-219">[211]</a></sup> The Republican campaign focused on continuing Reconstruction and restoring the public credit.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner2014340.2C_344_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner2014340.2C_344-220">[212]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Democrats nominated former New York Governor <a href="/wiki/Horatio_Seymour" title="Horatio Seymour">Horatio Seymour</a>. Their campaign focused mainly on ending Reconstruction and returning control of the South to the white planter class, which alienated many <a href="/wiki/War_Democrats" class="mw-redirect" title="War Democrats">War Democrats</a> in the North.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001468.E2.80.93469_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001468.E2.80.93469-221">[213]</a></sup> The Democrats attacked Reconstruction and the Republicans' support of African American rights, while deriding Grant, calling him captain of the "Black Marines".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981278.2C_283_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981278.2C_283-222">[214]</a></sup> Grant won the election by 300,000 votes out of 5,716,082 votes cast, receiving an <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">electoral college</a> landslide, of 214 votes to Seymour's 80. Grant, at the age of 46 was the youngest president elected at that time. His election was a triumph of principles that included sound money, efficient government, and the restoration of Southern reconstructed states.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001461_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001461-223">[215]</a></sup> Grant was the first president elected after the nation had outlawed slavery and granted citizenship to former slaves. Implementation of these new rights was slow to come; in the 1868 election, the black vote counted in only 16 of the 37 states, nearly all in the South.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981284_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981284-224">[216]</a></sup> Grant lost <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> primarily due to <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan" title="Ku Klux Klan">Ku Klux Klan</a> violence against African American voters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner2014243.E2.80.93244_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner2014243.E2.80.93244-225">[217]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_.281869.E2.80.9377.29">Presidency (1869–77)</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Presidency of Ulysses S. Grant">Presidency of Ulysses S. Grant</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:444px;max-width:444px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:170px;max-width:170px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulysses_S._Grant_seated_by_Brady_(cropped).tif" class="image"><img alt="Photographic portrait of Grant sitting by a table with open book and top hat on it" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Ulysses_S._Grant_seated_by_Brady_%28cropped%29.tif/lossy-page1-168px-Ulysses_S._Grant_seated_by_Brady_%28cropped%29.tif.jpg" width="168" height="215" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Ulysses_S._Grant_seated_by_Brady_%28cropped%29.tif/lossy-page1-252px-Ulysses_S._Grant_seated_by_Brady_%28cropped%29.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Ulysses_S._Grant_seated_by_Brady_%28cropped%29.tif/lossy-page1-336px-Ulysses_S._Grant_seated_by_Brady_%28cropped%29.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="850" data-file-height="1088" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">President Grant, 1869</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:270px;max-width:270px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulysses_Grant_and_Family_at_Long_Branch,_NJ_by_Pach_Brothers,_NY,_1870.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Family group on porch" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Ulysses_Grant_and_Family_at_Long_Branch%2C_NJ_by_Pach_Brothers%2C_NY%2C_1870.jpg/268px-Ulysses_Grant_and_Family_at_Long_Branch%2C_NJ_by_Pach_Brothers%2C_NY%2C_1870.jpg" width="268" height="218" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Ulysses_Grant_and_Family_at_Long_Branch%2C_NJ_by_Pach_Brothers%2C_NY%2C_1870.jpg/402px-Ulysses_Grant_and_Family_at_Long_Branch%2C_NJ_by_Pach_Brothers%2C_NY%2C_1870.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Ulysses_Grant_and_Family_at_Long_Branch%2C_NJ_by_Pach_Brothers%2C_NY%2C_1870.jpg/536px-Ulysses_Grant_and_Family_at_Long_Branch%2C_NJ_by_Pach_Brothers%2C_NY%2C_1870.jpg 2x" data-file-width="823" data-file-height="668" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The First Family: Ulysses and Julia Grant's family at the "summer capital" in <a href="/wiki/Long_Branch,_New_Jersey" title="Long Branch, New Jersey">Long Branch, New Jersey</a>, 1870</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On March 4, 1869, Grant was sworn in as the eighteenth President of the United States by Chief Justice <a href="/wiki/Salmon_P._Chase" title="Salmon P. Chase">Salmon P. Chase</a>. His presidency began with a break from tradition, as Johnson did not attend Grant's inauguration at the Capitol or ride with him as he departed the White House for the last time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981287.E2.80.93288_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981287.E2.80.93288-226">[218]</a></sup> Grant assumed the presidency with reluctance. In an 1869 letter to Sherman he wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I have been forced into it in spite of myself. I could not back down without, as it seems to me, leaving the contest for power for the next four years between mere trading politicians, the elevation of whom, no matter which party won, would lose to us, largely, the results of the costly war which we have gone through.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016463_218-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016463-218">[210]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In his inaugural address, Grant urged the ratification of the <a href="/wiki/Fifteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fifteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fifteenth Amendment</a> and said he would approach Reconstruction "calmly, without prejudice, hate or sectional pride."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatrick1968166_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatrick1968166-227">[219]</a></sup> He also recommended the "proper treatment" of <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a> be studied, advocating their civilization and eventual citizenship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981305_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981305-228">[220]</a></sup></p>
<p>Grant's unconventional <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant#Cabinet" title="Ulysses S. Grant">cabinet choices</a> sparked both disappointment and approval.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001465.E2.80.93466White2016475.2C_530_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001465.E2.80.93466White2016475.2C_530-229">[221]</a></sup> In his effort to create national harmony, Grant purposely avoided choosing <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a> leaders, selecting several non-politicians.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon2002246.E2.80.93247_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon2002246.E2.80.93247-230">[222]</a></sup> Out of loyalty to his friends, Grant chose <a href="/wiki/Elihu_B._Washburne" title="Elihu B. Washburne">Elihu B. Washburne</a> for Secretary of State and <a href="/wiki/John_A._Rawlins" class="mw-redirect" title="John A. Rawlins">John A. Rawlins</a> as Secretary of War.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon2002_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon2002-231">[223]</a></sup> Washburne was soon appointed minister to France and replaced by conservative New York statesman <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Fish" title="Hamilton Fish">Hamilton Fish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon2002_231-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon2002-231">[223]</a></sup> Rawlins died in office after serving only a few months and was replaced by <a href="/wiki/William_W._Belknap" title="William W. Belknap">William W. Belknap</a> of <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016507.2C_564_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016507.2C_564-232">[224]</a></sup> Grant's appointment for Secretary of Treasury, wealthy New York merchant <a href="/wiki/Alexander_T._Stewart" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexander T. Stewart">Alexander T. Stewart</a>, was found ineligible to hold office, and was replaced by Representative <a href="/wiki/George_S._Boutwell" title="George S. Boutwell">George S. Boutwell</a>, a Massachusetts <a href="/wiki/Radical_Republican" title="Radical Republican">Radical</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon2002_231-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon2002-231">[223]</a></sup> Grant's appointment for Secretary of Navy was Philadelphia businessman <a href="/wiki/Adolph_E._Borie" title="Adolph E. Borie">Adolph E. Borie</a>, but Borie quickly resigned having no interest in the department, and he was replaced by a relative unknown, <a href="/wiki/George_M._Robeson" title="George M. Robeson">George M. Robeson</a> of New Jersey.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon2002246.E2.80.93247_230-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon2002246.E2.80.93247-230">[222]</a></sup> His other cabinet appointments—<a href="/wiki/Jacob_D._Cox" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacob D. Cox">Jacob D. Cox</a> (Interior), <a href="/wiki/John_Creswell" title="John Creswell">John Creswell</a> (Postmaster General), and <a href="/wiki/Ebenezer_Rockwood_Hoar" class="mw-redirect" title="Ebenezer Rockwood Hoar">Ebenezer Rockwood Hoar</a> (Attorney General)—were well-received and uncontroversial.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001469.E2.80.93470_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001469.E2.80.93470-233">[225]</a></sup> Grant nominated Sherman his successor as general in chief and ordered Sherman be given control over War bureau chiefs and their departments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016474.E2.80.93475_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016474.E2.80.93475-234">[226]</a></sup> When Rawlins took over the War Department,<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[i]</a></sup> he complained to Grant that Sherman was given too much authority. Grant reluctantly revoked his own order, upsetting Sherman and damaging their wartime friendship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016474.E2.80.93475_234-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016474.E2.80.93475-234">[226]</a></sup></p>
<p>Grant also appointed four Justices to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a>: <a href="/wiki/William_Strong_(Pennsylvania_judge)" title="William Strong (Pennsylvania judge)">William Strong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_P._Bradley" title="Joseph P. Bradley">Joseph P. Bradley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ward_Hunt" title="Ward Hunt">Ward Hunt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice</a> <a href="/wiki/Morrison_Waite" title="Morrison Waite">Morrison Waite</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001507.E2.80.93508.2C_562.E2.80.93563_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001507.E2.80.93508.2C_562.E2.80.93563-237">[228]</a></sup> Hunt voted to uphold Reconstruction laws while Waite and Bradley did much to undermine them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981387.E2.80.93389_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981387.E2.80.93389-238">[229]</a></sup> To rectify his controversial General Order # 11 during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>, Grant appointed Jewish leaders to office, including <a href="/wiki/Simon_Wolf" title="Simon Wolf">Simon Wolf</a> recorder of deeds in Washington D.C., <a href="/wiki/Edward_S._Salomon" title="Edward S. Salomon">Edward S. Salomon</a> Governor of the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Territory" title="Washington Territory">Washington Territory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016494.E2.80.93495_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016494.E2.80.93495-239">[230]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_Reconstruction_and_civil_rights">Later Reconstruction and civil rights</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era" title="Reconstruction Era">Reconstruction Era</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Amos_T_Akerman_-_crop_and_minor_retouch.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Amos_T_Akerman_-_crop_and_minor_retouch.jpg/180px-Amos_T_Akerman_-_crop_and_minor_retouch.jpg" width="180" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Amos_T_Akerman_-_crop_and_minor_retouch.jpg/270px-Amos_T_Akerman_-_crop_and_minor_retouch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Amos_T_Akerman_-_crop_and_minor_retouch.jpg/360px-Amos_T_Akerman_-_crop_and_minor_retouch.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1952" data-file-height="2584" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Amos_T_Akerman_-_crop_and_minor_retouch.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Grant's Attorney General Amos T. Akerman vigorously prosecuted the Ku Klux Klan.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Unlike his predecessor, Grant's vision of <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era" title="Reconstruction Era">Reconstruction</a> included federal enforcement of civil rights and spoke out against voter intimidation of Southern blacks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoethals201596Kaczorowski1995155_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoethals201596Kaczorowski1995155-240">[231]</a></sup> In his message to Congress in 1874, Grant wrote, "Treat the negro as a citizen and a voter, as he is and must remain, and soon parties will be divided, not on the color line, but on principle."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016551_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016551-241">[232]</a></sup> He lobbied Congress to pass the Fifteenth Amendment, guaranteeing that no state could prevent someone from voting based on race, and believed that its passage would secure freedmen's rights. Grant asked Congress to admit representatives from the remaining unrepresented Southern states in conformity with Congressional Reconstruction; they did so, passing legislation providing that Mississippi, Virginia, and Texas would be represented in Congress after they ratified the <a href="/wiki/Fifteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fifteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fifteenth Amendment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a435_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a435-242">[233]</a></sup> Grant pressured Congress to draw up legislation that would seat African American state legislators in Georgia, who had been ousted by white conservatives. Congress responded through legislation; the members were re-seated in the Georgia legislature, and Georgia was required to adopt the Fifteenth Amendment to gain representation in Congress. By July 1870, the four remaining states were readmitted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a465_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a465-243">[234]</a></sup></p>
<p>To bolster the new amendment, Grant relied on the army and in 1870 he signed legislation creating the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Justice_Department" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Justice Department">Justice Department</a>, primarily to enforce federal laws in the South. Where the attorney general had once been only a legal adviser to the president, he now led a cabinet department dedicated to enforcing federal law, including a <a href="/wiki/Solicitor_General_of_the_United_States" title="Solicitor General of the United States">solicitor general</a> to argue on the government's behalf in court.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001543.E2.80.93545_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001543.E2.80.93545-244">[235]</a></sup> Under Grant's first attorney general, <a href="/wiki/Ebenezer_R._Hoar" title="Ebenezer R. Hoar">Ebenezer R. Hoar</a>, the administration was not especially aggressive in prosecuting white Southerners who terrorized their black neighbors, but Hoar's successor, <a href="/wiki/Amos_T._Akerman" title="Amos T. Akerman">Amos T. Akerman</a>, was more zealous. Alarmed by a rise in terror by the <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan" title="Ku Klux Klan">Ku Klux Klan</a> and other groups against African Americans, Congress (with Grant's encouragement) passed a series of laws, the <a href="/wiki/Enforcement_Acts" title="Enforcement Acts">Enforcement Acts</a> from 1870 to 1871, which made depriving African Americans their civil rights a federal offense and authorized the president to use the military to enforce the laws.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981368.E2.80.93369Brands2012a467.2C_470_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981368.E2.80.93369Brands2012a467.2C_470-245">[236]</a></sup> In May 1871, Grant ordered federal troops to help <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marshals_Service" title="United States Marshals Service">marshals</a> in arresting Klansmen. That October, on Akerman's recommendation, Grant suspended <i><a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">habeas corpus</a></i> in part of South Carolina and sent federal troops to enforce the law there. After prosecutions by Akerman and his replacement, <a href="/wiki/George_Henry_Williams" title="George Henry Williams">George Henry Williams</a>, the Klan's power collapsed; by 1872, elections in the South saw African Americans voting in record numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001544.E2.80.93547_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001544.E2.80.93547-246">[237]</a></sup> That same year, Grant signed the <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_Act" title="Amnesty Act">Amnesty Act</a>, which restored political rights to former Confederates. Lacking sufficient funding, the Justice Department stopped prosecutions of the Klan in June 1873, and Grant offered the Klan clemency in exchange for peace.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaczorowski1995182_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaczorowski1995182-247">[238]</a></sup> The Justice Department's civil rights prosecutions continued throughout Grant's second term but with fewer yearly cases and convictions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang1997102_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang1997102-248">[239]</a></sup> Additionally, Grant's Postmaster General <a href="/wiki/John_Creswell" title="John Creswell">John Creswell</a>, using his patronage powers, integrated the postal system and appointed African American postmasters across the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsborneBombaro201554_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsborneBombaro201554-249">[240]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harpers1874LouisianaOutrage.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Harpers1874LouisianaOutrage.jpg/220px-Harpers1874LouisianaOutrage.jpg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Harpers1874LouisianaOutrage.jpg/330px-Harpers1874LouisianaOutrage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Harpers1874LouisianaOutrage.jpg/440px-Harpers1874LouisianaOutrage.jpg 2x" data-file-width="961" data-file-height="740" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harpers1874LouisianaOutrage.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Picture of mobs rioting, wearing uniforms and using weapons on a city street, entitled "The Louisiana Outrage"; White Leaguers attacking the integrated police force and state militia, New Orleans, September 1874</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the Klan's decline, other conservative whites formed armed groups, such as the <a href="/wiki/Red_Shirts_(Southern_United_States)" title="Red Shirts (Southern United States)">Red Shirts</a> and the <a href="/wiki/White_League" title="White League">White League</a> who openly used violence and intimidation to take control of state governments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERable2007144.E2.80.93186_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERable2007144.E2.80.93186-250">[241]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1873" title="Panic of 1873">Panic of 1873</a> and the ensuing depression contributed to public fatigue, and the North grew less concerned with Reconstruction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001552.E2.80.93553_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001552.E2.80.93553-251">[242]</a></sup> The Freedman's Savings Bank, whose primary depositors were <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African Americans</a>, collapsed in the ensuing economic turmoil.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974140_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974140-252">[243]</a></sup> Supreme Court rulings in the <i><a href="/wiki/Slaughter-House_Cases" title="Slaughter-House Cases">Slaughter-House Cases</a></i> (1873) and <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Cruikshank" title="United States v. Cruikshank">United States v. Cruikshank</a></i> (1875) restricted federal enforcement of civil rights.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaczorowski1995184_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaczorowski1995184-253">[244]</a></sup> Grant began to limit the use of troops to avoid the impression that he was acting as a military dictator; he was also concerned that increased military pressure in the South might cause conservative whites in the North to bolt the Republican Party. In 1874, Grant ended the <a href="/wiki/Brooks%E2%80%93Baxter_War" title="Brooks–Baxter War">Brooks–Baxter War</a> bringing Reconstruction in <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a> to a peaceful conclusion, but that same year, he sent troops and warships under Major General <a href="/wiki/William_H._Emory" title="William H. Emory">William H. Emory</a> to New Orleans in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Colfax_Massacre" class="mw-redirect" title="Colfax Massacre">Colfax Massacre</a> and disputes over the election of Governor <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt_Kellogg" title="William Pitt Kellogg">William Pitt Kellogg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a538.E2.80.93541Foner2014528_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a538.E2.80.93541Foner2014528-254">[245]</a></sup> Emory peacefully restored Kellogg to office and the following year the parties reached a compromise allowing Democrats to retain control of the Louisiana House.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENystrom2010176Foner2014555_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENystrom2010176Foner2014555-255">[246]</a></sup> Under public pressure, Grant recalled Sheridan and most of the federal troops from Louisiana.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a553_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a553-256">[247]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1875, Democratic "<a href="/wiki/Redeemers" title="Redeemers">Redeemer</a>" politicians took control of all but three Southern states. As violence against black Southerners escalated once more, <a href="/wiki/Edwards_Pierrepont" title="Edwards Pierrepont">Edwards Pierrepont</a> (Grant's fourth attorney general) told Governor <a href="/wiki/Adelbert_Ames" title="Adelbert Ames">Adelbert Ames</a> of Mississippi that the people were "tired of the autumnal outbreaks in the South", and declined to intervene directly, instead, sending an emissary to negotiate a peaceful election.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981420.E2.80.93422_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981420.E2.80.93422-257">[248]</a></sup> Grant signed an ambitious <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1875" title="Civil Rights Act of 1875">Civil Rights Act of 1875</a>, which expanded federal law enforcement by prohibiting discrimination on account of race in <a href="/wiki/Public_accommodations" title="Public accommodations">public lodging</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_transport" title="Public transport">public transportation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jury_selection_in_the_United_States" title="Jury selection in the United States">jury service</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a553_256-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a553-256">[247]</a></sup> However, it did not stop the rise of white supremacist forces in the South.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981418.E2.80.93419_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981418.E2.80.93419-258">[249]</a></sup> In October 1876, Grant sent troops to South Carolina to aid Republican Governor <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Henry_Chamberlain" title="Daniel Henry Chamberlain">Daniel Chamberlain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a570_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a570-259">[250]</a></sup> Even so, the remaining three Republican governments in the South fell to Redeemers after the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1876" title="United States presidential election, 1876">1876 presidential election</a>, and the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Compromise_of_1877" title="Compromise of 1877">Compromise of 1877</a> marked the end of Reconstruction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001603.E2.80.93604_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001603.E2.80.93604-260">[251]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">[j]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_peace_policy">Indian peace policy</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/American_Indian_Wars#West_of_the_Mississippi_.281811.E2.80.931924.29" title="American Indian Wars">American Indian Wars §&#160;West of the Mississippi (1811–1924)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ely_S._Parker.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Formal photographic portrait of a sitting mustached man" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ely_S._Parker.jpg/180px-Ely_S._Parker.jpg" width="180" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ely_S._Parker.jpg/270px-Ely_S._Parker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ely_S._Parker.jpg/360px-Ely_S._Parker.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1175" data-file-height="1421" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ely_S._Parker.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ely_S._Parker" title="Ely S. Parker">Ely S. Parker</a>, appointed by President Grant.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When Grant took office in 1869, the nation's policy towards Indians was in chaos, with more than 250,000 Indians being governed by 370 treaties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016487_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016487-64">[61]</a></sup> Grant's presidency marked a radical reform in Indian policy while promising in his inaugural address "the proper treatment of the original occupants of this land—the Indians."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016487_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016487-64">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">[k]</a></sup> Grant appointed <a href="/wiki/Ely_S._Parker" title="Ely S. Parker">Ely S. Parker</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Seneca_people" title="Seneca people">Seneca</a> Indian, former member of his wartime staff, as <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Indian_Affairs" title="Bureau of Indian Affairs">Commissioner of Indian Affairs</a>, the first Native American to serve in this position. With his familiarity of Indian life, Parker became the chief architect of Grant's Peace policy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016490.E2.80.93491Simon2002250Smith2001472.E2.80.93473_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016490.E2.80.93491Simon2002250Smith2001472.E2.80.93473-264">[253]</a></sup> Indian matters were given priority by Congress during Grant's first months in office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981312_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981312-265">[254]</a></sup> In April, Grant signed a law establishing a <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Indian_Commissioners" title="Board of Indian Commissioners">Board of Indian Commissioners</a> to oversee spending and reduce corruption in the Bureau of Indian Affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001535Simon2002250_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001535Simon2002250-266">[255]</a></sup> In 1871, Grant signed a bill ending the Indian treaty system; the law now treated individual Native Americans as <a href="/wiki/Ward_(law)" title="Ward (law)">wards</a> of the federal government, and no longer dealt with the tribes as sovereign entities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaltmann1971327_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaltmann1971327-267">[256]</a></sup> Grant believed that Indians, given opportunities for education and work, could serve alongside white men.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016491_262-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016491-262">[252]</a></sup> Grant's Peace Policy aimed to replace entrepreneurs serving as Indian agents with missionaries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981308.E2.80.93309Brands2012a502_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981308.E2.80.93309Brands2012a502-268">[257]</a></sup> Grant wished for Indians to be protected on reservations and educated in farming, abandoning their hunter-gatherer way of life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981308.E2.80.93309Brands2012a502_268-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981308.E2.80.93309Brands2012a502-268">[257]</a></sup> "My efforts in the future will be directed," Grant said in his second inaugural address, "by a humane course, to bring the aborigines of the country under the benign influences of education and civilization&#160;... Wars of extermination&#160;... are demoralizing and wicked." Although, as biographer <a href="/wiki/Jean_Edward_Smith" title="Jean Edward Smith">Jean Edward Smith</a> wrote, Grant's peace policy was "remarkably progressive and humanitarian" for its time, it ultimately disregarded native cultures, something modern Americans see "as a grave error."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001541_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001541-269">[258]</a></sup></p>
<p>The slaughter of millions the <a href="/wiki/American_bison" title="American bison">buffalo</a>, encouraged by Grant's military subordinates, led to conflict with the <a href="/wiki/Plains_Indians" title="Plains Indians">Plains Indians</a>, who needed the buffalo for food and their tribal religion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001536.E2.80.93538Brands2012a501.E2.80.93503_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001536.E2.80.93538Brands2012a501.E2.80.93503-270">[259]</a></sup> In 1874, Grant pocket vetoed a bill to protect the bison, supporting Secretary of Interior <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Delano" title="Columbus Delano">Columbus Delano</a>, who believed correctly the slaughter of bison would force Plains Indians to abandon their nomadic lifestyle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor20113187.E2.80.933188_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor20113187.E2.80.933188-271">[260]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPritchard19995_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPritchard19995-272">[261]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">[l]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sioux" title="Sioux">Sioux</a> and other Plains tribes accepted the reservation system, but encounters with whites in search of <a href="/wiki/Black_Hills_Gold_Rush" title="Black Hills Gold Rush">gold in the Black Hills</a> led to <a href="/wiki/Great_Sioux_War_of_1876" title="Great Sioux War of 1876">renewed war</a> by the end of Grant's second term, ending the understanding that had developed between Grant and Sioux Chief <a href="/wiki/Red_Cloud" title="Red Cloud">Red Cloud</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a501.E2.80.93503McFeely1981436.E2.80.93437_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a501.E2.80.93503McFeely1981436.E2.80.93437-275">[263]</a></sup> Under Major Generals <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Otis_Howard" title="Oliver Otis Howard">Oliver Otis Howard</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Crook" title="George Crook">George Crook</a>, Grant's policy had greater success in the Southwest. Howard, the former head of the Freedmen's Bureau, negotiated peace with the <a href="/wiki/Apache" title="Apache">Apache</a> in 1872, convincing their leader, <a href="/wiki/Cochise" title="Cochise">Cochise</a>, to move the tribe to a new reservation, and ending <a href="/wiki/Apache_Wars" title="Apache Wars">a war</a> started the year before. In Oregon, relations were less peaceful, as <a href="/wiki/Modoc_War" title="Modoc War">war with the Modocs</a> erupted in April 1873. The <a href="/wiki/Modoc_people" title="Modoc people">Modocs</a> refused to move to a reservation and killed the local army commander, Major General <a href="/wiki/Edward_Canby" title="Edward Canby">Edward Canby</a>. Although disturbed by Canby's death, Grant ordered restraint, disregarding Sherman's advice to seek revenge or exterminate the tribe. The army captured, tried, and executed the four Modoc warriors responsible for Canby's murder in October 1873. Grant ordered the rest of the Modoc tribe relocated to the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Territory" title="Indian Territory">Indian Territory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001532.E2.80.93535_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001532.E2.80.93535-276">[264]</a></sup> In 1874, the army defeated the <a href="/wiki/Comanche" title="Comanche">Comanche</a> Indians at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Palo_Duro_Canyon" title="Battle of Palo Duro Canyon">Battle of Palo Duro Canyon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoffey2011604_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoffey2011604-277">[265]</a></sup> Though Indian casualties were low, their villages were burned and horses slaughtered, eventually forcing them to finally settle at the <a href="/wiki/Fort_Sill" title="Fort Sill">Fort Sill</a> reservation in 1875.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoffey2011604.E2.80.93605_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoffey2011604.E2.80.93605-278">[266]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Great_Sioux_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Sioux War">Great Sioux War</a>, Grant came into conflict with Colonel <a href="/wiki/George_Armstrong_Custer" title="George Armstrong Custer">George Armstrong Custer</a> after Custer testified in 1876 about corruption in the War Department. Grant ordered Custer arrested for breach of military protocol and barred him from leading an upcoming campaign against the Sioux. Grant later relented and let Custer fight under Brigadier General <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Terry" title="Alfred Terry">Alfred Terry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDonovan2008115.2C_322.E2.80.93323_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDonovan2008115.2C_322.E2.80.93323-279">[267]</a></sup> Sioux warriors led by <a href="/wiki/Crazy_Horse" title="Crazy Horse">Crazy Horse</a> killed Custer at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Little_Big_Horn" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of the Little Big Horn">Battle of the Little Big Horn</a>, the army's most famous defeat in the Indian wars. Two months later, Grant castigated Custer in the press, saying "I regard Custer's massacre as a sacrifice of troops, brought on by Custer himself, that was wholly unnecessary – wholly unnecessary."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a565.E2.80.93566_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a565.E2.80.93566-280">[268]</a></sup> Custer's death shocked the nation, leading Congress to appropriate funds for more troops, two more Western forts and barred Indians from purchasing weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDonovan2008115.2C_322.E2.80.93323_279-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDonovan2008115.2C_322.E2.80.93323-279">[267]</a></sup> In spite of Grant's efforts, over 200 battles were fought with the Indians during his presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981316_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981316-281">[269]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_affairs">Foreign affairs</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Annexation_of_Santo_Domingo" title="Annexation of Santo Domingo">Annexation of Santo Domingo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Washington_(1871)" title="Treaty of Washington (1871)">Treaty of Washington (1871)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Virginius_Affair" title="Virginius Affair">Virginius Affair</a></div>
<div class="mw-stack mobile-float-reset" style="box-sizing: border-box; float:left;">
<div style="overflow: hidden; margin: 1px;">
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hamilton_Fish_Brady_Edited.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Formal photographic portrait of bearded man" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Hamilton_Fish_Brady_Edited.jpg/170px-Hamilton_Fish_Brady_Edited.jpg" width="170" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Hamilton_Fish_Brady_Edited.jpg/255px-Hamilton_Fish_Brady_Edited.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Hamilton_Fish_Brady_Edited.jpg/340px-Hamilton_Fish_Brady_Edited.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1094" data-file-height="1506" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hamilton_Fish_Brady_Edited.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center><a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Fish" title="Hamilton Fish">Hamilton Fish</a><br />
Secretary of State, 1869–1877</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The most pressing foreign policy concerns when Grant took office were resolving the <a href="/wiki/Alabama_Claims" title="Alabama Claims"><i>Alabama</i> claims</a> against Great Britain and whether to recognize <a href="/wiki/Ten_Years%27_War" title="Ten Years' War">Cuban belligerency</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon2002249Smith2001491_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon2002249Smith2001491-282">[270]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">[m]</a></sup> The dispute with the United Kingdom stemmed from a complex of grievances centering on attacks on American shipping during the Civil War by the <a href="/wiki/CSS_Alabama" title="CSS Alabama">CSS&#160;<i>Alabama</i></a>, a Confederate warship constructed in England.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981334.E2.80.93336Simon2002249_285-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981334.E2.80.93336Simon2002249-285">[272]</a></sup> Senator <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sumner" title="Charles Sumner">Charles Sumner</a>, Chairman of the Foreign Relations Committee, believed the British had violated American neutrality and demanded reparations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981352.E2.80.93354Simon2002249_286-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981352.E2.80.93354Simon2002249-286">[273]</a></sup> Sumner convinced his fellow Senators to reject the Johnson administration's proposed settlement, believing that Britain should directly pay $2 billion in gold or, alternatively, cede Canada to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001508Simon2002249_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001508Simon2002249-287">[274]</a></sup> Fish and Boutwell convinced Grant that peaceful relations with Britain were more important than acquisition of territory, and the two nations agreed to negotiate along those lines.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001508.E2.80.93511_288-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001508.E2.80.93511-288">[275]</a></sup> To avoid jeopardizing negotiations, Grant refrained from recognizing Cuban rebels who were fighting for independence from Spain, which would have been inconsistent with American objections to the British granting belligerent status to Confederates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon2002249_283-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon2002249-283">[271]</a></sup> A commission in Washington produced a treaty whereby an international tribunal would settle the damage amounts; the British admitted regret, but not fault.<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">[n]</a></sup> The Senate approved the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Washington_(1871)" title="Treaty of Washington (1871)">Treaty of Washington</a>, which also settled disputes over fishing rights and maritime boundaries, by a 50–12 vote in 1871.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001512.E2.80.93515_289-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001512.E2.80.93515-289">[276]</a></sup></p>
<p>Grant's success with Britain was undermined by his attempt to annex the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a>, an independent nation unable to pay its debt. Many Americans believed a <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean</a> naval base would establish naval dominance and be useful in protecting shipping from piracy in a potential isthmian canal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon2002249_283-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon2002249-283">[271]</a></sup> Anti-imperialist Republicans had previously rejected a Johnson administration treaty to establish a <a href="/wiki/Saman%C3%A1_Bay" title="Samaná Bay">Samaná Bay</a> naval base.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981336.E2.80.93338Brands2012a452White2016507_291-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981336.E2.80.93338Brands2012a452White2016507-291">[277]</a></sup> Grant took interest in Dominican annexation and sent his secretary, <a href="/wiki/Orville_E._Babcock" title="Orville E. Babcock">Orville E.
