Babcock</a>, there to consult with <a href="/wiki/Buenaventura_B%C3%A1ez" title="Buenaventura Báez">Buenaventura Báez</a>, the pro-annexation Dominican president.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016McFeely1981336.E2.80.93338McFeely1974137_292-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016McFeely1981336.E2.80.93338McFeely1974137-292">[278]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">[o]</a></sup> Babcock returned in September 1869 with a draft treaty of annexation, although Fish had not given him any diplomatic authority.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001500.E2.80.93502McFeely1974137White2016508_295-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001500.E2.80.93502McFeely1974137White2016508-295">[280]</a></sup> Given such authority by Fish, Babcock visited the island nation a second time making a treaty for Dominican annexation and the lease of Samaná Bay.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001500.E2.80.93502White2016508Simon2002249_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001500.E2.80.93502White2016508Simon2002249-296">[281]</a></sup> The cabinet discussed the treaties at a meeting on December 21.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016_297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016-297">[282]</a></sup> Fish dismissed annexation, seeing the island as politically unstable.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001500.E2.80.93502_298-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001500.E2.80.93502-298">[283]</a></sup> Grant supported annexation believing acquisition of the majority-black nation would increase U.S. commerce, create a refuge for African Americans, and help to exploit the island's natural resources.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974139Brands2012a455.E2.80.93456_299-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974139Brands2012a455.E2.80.93456-299">[284]</a></sup> He lobbied Sumner in hopes of influencing him to help with passage of the treaties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981339.E2.80.93341_300-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981339.E2.80.93341-300">[285]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016509.E2.80.93511_301-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016509.E2.80.93511-301">[286]</a></sup> Fish added to the effort out of loyalty to Grant, but to no avail; Sumner was set against annexation and on June 30, 1870, the Senate rejected the treaties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016511.E2.80.93512Pletcher1998167_302-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016511.E2.80.93512Pletcher1998167-302">[287]</a></sup> A congressional investigation headed by Senator <a href="/wiki/Carl_Schurz" title="Carl Schurz">Carl Schurz</a> revealed speculators had promoted the treaties' passage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974138_303-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974138-303">[288]</a></sup> Unwilling to admit defeat, Grant convinced Congress to send a commission (including <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Douglass" title="Frederick Douglass">Frederick Douglass</a>) to investigate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a461_304-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a461-304">[289]</a></sup> Although the commission approved Grant's call for annexation in its findings, the Senate remained opposed and Grant was forced to abandon further efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a461Smith2001505.E2.80.93506_305-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a461Smith2001505.E2.80.93506-305">[290]</a></sup> Grant retaliated by firing Sumner's friend and Minister to Great Britain, <a href="/wiki/John_Lothrop_Motley" title="John Lothrop Motley">John Lothrop Motley</a>, while he pressured the Senate to depose Sumner of his chairmanship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon2002250McFeely1981349.E2.80.93352_306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon2002250McFeely1981349.E2.80.93352-306">[291]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kalakaua_Grant_state_visit_1874.jpg" class="image"><img alt="reception line" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Kalakaua_Grant_state_visit_1874.jpg/220px-Kalakaua_Grant_state_visit_1874.jpg" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Kalakaua_Grant_state_visit_1874.jpg/330px-Kalakaua_Grant_state_visit_1874.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Kalakaua_Grant_state_visit_1874.jpg/440px-Kalakaua_Grant_state_visit_1874.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7393" data-file-height="5440" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kalakaua_Grant_state_visit_1874.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
King <a href="/wiki/Kal%C4%81kaua" title="Kalākaua">Kalākaua</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hawaii" title="Kingdom of Hawaii">Hawaii</a> meets President Grant at the White House in 1874</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In October 1873, Grant's neutrality policy was shaken, when a Spanish cruiser captured a merchant ship, <a href="/wiki/Virginius_Affair" title="Virginius Affair"><i>Virginius</i></a>, flying the U.S. flag, carrying <a href="/wiki/Materiel" title="Materiel">war materials</a> and men to aid the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Years%27_War" title="Ten Years' War">Cuban insurrection</a>. Spanish authorities executed the prisoners, including eight American citizens, and many Americans called for war with Spain. Grant ordered the Navy to increase its presence in the Caribbean. Fish, with Grant's support, worked to reach a peaceful resolution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016506_307-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016506-307">[292]</a></sup> Spain's president, <a href="/wiki/Emilio_Castelar_y_Ripoll" class="mw-redirect" title="Emilio Castelar y Ripoll">Emilio Castelar y Ripoll</a>, expressed regret for the tragedy, surrendered the <i>Virginius</i> and paid a cash indemnity of $80,000 to the families of the executed Americans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENevins1936667.E2.80.93669.2C_682_308-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENevins1936667.E2.80.93669.2C_682-308">[293]</a></sup> Realizing the Navy was susceptible to European naval powers, in June 1874, Secretary Robeson commissioned the reconstruction of five redesigned double-turreted <a href="/wiki/Monitor_(warship)" title="Monitor (warship)">monitor</a> warships.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriedman1985405.E2.80.93406_309-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriedman1985405.E2.80.93406-309">[294]</a></sup></p>
<p>The administration's diplomacy was also at work in the Pacific. In December 1874, Grant held a state dinner at the White House for the King of Hawaii, <a href="/wiki/David_Kalakaua" class="mw-redirect" title="David Kalakaua">David Kalakaua</a>, who was seeking duty-free sugar importation to the US. Grant and Fish secured a <a href="/wiki/Reciprocity_Treaty_of_1875" title="Reciprocity Treaty of 1875">free trade treaty in 1875</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hawaii" title="Kingdom of Hawaii">Kingdom of Hawaii</a>, incorporating the Pacific islands' sugar industry into the United States' economic sphere.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKreiser201319_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKreiser201319-310">[295]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gold_standard_and_the_Gold_Ring">Gold standard and the Gold Ring</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Black_Friday_(1869)" title="Black Friday (1869)">Black Friday (1869)</a></div>
<p>Soon after taking office, Grant took steps to return the nation's currency to a more secure footing. During the Civil War, Congress had authorized the Treasury to issue banknotes that, unlike the rest of the currency, were not backed by gold or silver. The "<a href="/wiki/United_States_Note" title="United States Note">greenback</a>" notes, as they were known, were necessary to pay the unprecedented war debts, but they also caused inflation and forced gold-backed money out of circulation; Grant determined to return the national <a href="/wiki/Economy" title="Economy">economy</a> to pre-war monetary standards.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981279_311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981279-311">[296]</a></sup> On March 18, Grant signed into law the <a href="/wiki/Public_Credit_Act_of_1869" title="Public Credit Act of 1869">Public Credit Act of 1869</a> that guaranteed bondholders would be repaid in "coin or its equivalent"; while greenbacks would gradually be redeemed by the Treasury and replaced by notes backed by specie, the act committed the government to full return of the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a> within ten years.<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">[297]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016476.E2.80.93478_313-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016476.E2.80.93478-313">[298]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">[p]</a></sup> To strengthen the dollar, Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/George_S._Boutwell" title="George S. Boutwell">George S. Boutwell</a>, backed by Grant, sold gold from the Treasury bi-weekly and bought back high-interest Treasury bonds issued during the war; this had the effect of reducing the deficit, but deflating the currency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001480.E2.80.93481_316-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001480.E2.80.93481-316">[300]</a></sup> By September 1, Boutwell had reduced the <a href="/wiki/National_debt_of_the_United_States" title="National debt of the United States">national debt</a> by $50 million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016482_317-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016482-317">[301]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_Thwarting_the_Gold_Ring_1869.tif" class="image"><img alt="Cartoon, entitled &quot;The 'Boy of the Period' Stirring up the Animals', shows Fisk poking bulls and bears and a man running toward them with a bag in his hand" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Grant_Thwarting_the_Gold_Ring_1869.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Grant_Thwarting_the_Gold_Ring_1869.tif.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Grant_Thwarting_the_Gold_Ring_1869.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Grant_Thwarting_the_Gold_Ring_1869.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Grant_Thwarting_the_Gold_Ring_1869.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Grant_Thwarting_the_Gold_Ring_1869.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5764" data-file-height="4062" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_Thwarting_the_Gold_Ring_1869.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A cartoon showing Jim Fisk stirring up the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Gold_Exchange" title="New York Gold Exchange">gold market</a> in New York while Grant releases treasury gold to defeat the Gold Ring.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The efforts undertook to control the gold market and help the national economy set the stage for a major financial scandal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001482_318-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001482-318">[302]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974134.E2.80.93135_319-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974134.E2.80.93135-319">[303]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jay_Gould" title="Jay Gould">Jay Gould</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">Wall Street</a> trader and railroad magnate, and financier <a href="/wiki/James_Fisk_(financier)" title="James Fisk (financier)">Jim Fisk</a>, seeking to drive up the gold price, enlisted the help of another speculator <a href="/wiki/Abel_Corbin" title="Abel Corbin">Abel Corbin</a>, Grant's brother-in-law, who used his connection with the president to get inside information (the collaborators were later known as the "Gold Ring.")<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a437.E2.80.93443McFeely1974134_320-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a437.E2.80.93443McFeely1974134-320">[304]</a></sup> Corbin convinced Grant to appoint a Gould associate, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Butterfield" title="Daniel Butterfield">Daniel Butterfield</a>, as assistant Treasurer, where he could gather information for the Ring.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981136.2C_323.E2.80.93324_321-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981136.2C_323.E2.80.93324-321">[305]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Gould and Fisk quietly stockpiled gold. Gould convinced Corbin that a high gold price would be good for the nation's prosperity, and Corbin passed this theory on to Grant, who allowed the Treasury to act accordingly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974134_322-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974134-322">[306]</a></sup> After consulting in early September with <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Turney_Stewart" title="Alexander Turney Stewart">Alexander Stewart</a> (his erstwhile nominee for Treasury Secretary), Grant stopped the sale of gold, believing a higher gold price would help Western farmers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a437.E2.80.93443_323-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a437.E2.80.93443-323">[307]</a></sup></p>
<p>By mid-September, Grant warned Boutwell to be on his guard as the gold price continued to rise, while the conspirators bought ever more and the rising price affected the wider economy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974134Smith2001486.E2.80.93489_324-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974134Smith2001486.E2.80.93489-324">[308]</a></sup> Grant, seeing that the increase was unnatural, told Boutwell to sell gold, which reduced its price.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001486.E2.80.93489_325-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001486.E2.80.93489-325">[309]</a></sup> Boutwell did so the next day, on September 24, 1869, later known as <a href="/wiki/Black_Friday_(1869)" title="Black Friday (1869)">Black Friday</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974135_326-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974135-326">[310]</a></sup> The sale of gold from the Treasury defeated Gould's scheme as the gold price plummeted, relieving the economic tension.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a445.2C_636_327-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a445.2C_636-327">[311]</a></sup> Many brokerage firms collapsed while trade volume and agriculture prices plummeted, causing a mild recession, but by January 1870, the economy resumed its post-war recovery.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981328Smith2001490_328-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981328Smith2001490-328">[312]</a></sup> Gould and Fisk managed to escape without much harm to themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001490_329-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001490-329">[313]</a></sup> A Congressional investigation followed, chaired by <a href="/wiki/James_A._Garfield" title="James A. Garfield">James A. Garfield</a> and cleared Grant of wrongdoing, but excoriated Gould and Fisk for their manipulation of the gold market and Corbin for exploiting his personal connection to Grant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a445.E2.80.93446_330-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a445.E2.80.93446-330">[314]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1872_and_second_term">Election of 1872 and second term</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1872" title="United States presidential election, 1872">United States presidential election, 1872</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_Liberal_Gratitude.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A Thomas Nast cartoon depicting Grant steering a ship and being challenged by opponents during presidential election of 1872." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Grant_Liberal_Gratitude.jpg/220px-Grant_Liberal_Gratitude.jpg" width="220" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Grant_Liberal_Gratitude.jpg/330px-Grant_Liberal_Gratitude.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Grant_Liberal_Gratitude.jpg/440px-Grant_Liberal_Gratitude.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="1097" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_Liberal_Gratitude.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cartoon by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Nast" title="Thomas Nast">Thomas Nast</a> on Grant's opponents in the reelection campaign</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite his administration's scandals, Grant continued to be personally popular.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner2014499.E2.80.93500_331-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner2014499.E2.80.93500-331">[315]</a></sup> His reelection was supported by Frederick Douglas and other prominent abolitionists along with reformers of the Indian question.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016535_332-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016535-332">[316]</a></sup> A growing number of political reformers, however, were disappointed by the Gold Ring, Grant's support of Reconstruction and <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_presidential_administration_scandals#New_York_custom_house_ring" title="Ulysses S. Grant presidential administration scandals">corruption</a> in the New York Customs House.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner2014500.E2.80.93507_333-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner2014500.E2.80.93507-333">[317]</a></sup> To placate them, Grant created a <a href="/wiki/Civil_Service_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil Service Commission">Civil Service Commission</a> authorized by Congress in 1871.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatrick1968172_334-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatrick1968172-334">[318]</a></sup> The Commission, chaired by reformer <a href="/wiki/George_William_Curtis" title="George William Curtis">George William Curtis</a>, proposed reformist rules and regulations, which Grant implemented by executive order in April 1872, Congress appropriating funds in May.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon2002250_335-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon2002250-335">[319]</a></sup> Congress stopped funding the Commission in December 1875, having refused to pass legislation to implement its recommendations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001589.E2.80.93590Patrick1968173_336-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001589.E2.80.93590Patrick1968173-336">[320]</a></sup> There was further intraparty division between the faction most concerned with the plight of the freedmen and that concerned with the growth of industry. During the war, both factions' interests had aligned, and in 1868 both had supported Grant. As the wartime coalition began to fray, Grant's alignment with the party's pro-Reconstruction elements alienated party leaders who favored an end to federal intervention in Southern racial issues.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a488.E2.80.93489_337-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a488.E2.80.93489-337">[321]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many of that faction split from the party in 1872, calling themselves the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Liberal Republican Party (United States)">Liberal Republican Party</a>. Led by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Francis_Adams_Sr." title="Charles Francis Adams Sr.">Charles Francis Adams</a> of Massachusetts and Senator <a href="/wiki/Carl_Schurz" title="Carl Schurz">Carl Schurz</a> of Missouri, they denounced the patronage system that Sumner, a Liberal Republican sympathizer, called "<a href="/wiki/Grantism" title="Grantism">Grantism</a>"; opposed a strong federal government; and demanded amnesty for Confederate soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang1997103_338-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang1997103-338">[322]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDiNunzio1973367.E2.80.93370_339-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDiNunzio1973367.E2.80.93370-339">[323]</a></sup> The Liberal Republicans distrusted black suffrage and demanded literacy tests for voting while opposing federal enforcement of equal voting rights in the South.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang1997103.E2.80.93104_340-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang1997103.E2.80.93104-340">[324]</a></sup> They nominated <a href="/wiki/Horace_Greeley" title="Horace Greeley">Horace Greeley</a>, another Republican who had come to dislike Grant and his policies, with Governor <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Gratz_Brown" title="Benjamin Gratz Brown">Benjamin Gratz Brown</a> of Missouri nominated for Vice President.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a494_341-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a494-341">[325]</a></sup> Democrats, seeking to benefit from anti-Grant sentiment, nominated Greeley as well.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a495_342-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a495-342">[326]</a></sup> The rest of the Republican Party nominated Grant for reelection, with Senator <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wilson" title="Henry Wilson">Henry Wilson</a> of Massachusetts replacing Colfax as vice-presidential nominee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981381_343-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981381-343">[327]</a></sup> Wilson, viewed as a practical reformer and civil rights advocate, was meant to strengthen the Republican ticket, but the <a href="/wiki/Cr%C3%A9dit_Mobilier_of_America_scandal" title="Crédit Mobilier of America scandal">Crédit Mobilier scandal</a> involving a railroad company that bribed many Congressmen ensnared both Colfax and Wilson, but not Grant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a508.E2.80.93509White2016539_344-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a508.E2.80.93509White2016539-344">[328]</a></sup> To the Liberals' chagrin, Greeley made Grant's Southern policy, rather than reform, the main campaign issue.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner2014509_345-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner2014509-345">[329]</a></sup> The fusion effort failed and Grant was easily reelected. Liberal Republicans were unable to deliver many votes, and Greeley was only successful in areas the Democrats would have carried without him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981384_346-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981384-346">[330]</a></sup> A strong economy, debt reduction, lowered tariffs, repeal of the income tax, and civil service reforms helped Grant defeat Greeley.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon2002250_335-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon2002250-335">[319]</a></sup> Grant won 56 percent of the popular vote and an Electoral College landslide of 286 to 66.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a499_347-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a499-347">[331]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-348" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-348">[q]</a></sup> A majority of African Americans in the South voted for Grant, while Democratic opposition remained mostly peaceful.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner2014508_349-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner2014508-349">[332]</a></sup> Grant lost in six former slave states that wanted to see an end to Reconstruction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoethals201598_350-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoethals201598-350">[333]</a></sup></p>
<p>Grant was sworn in for his second term by <a href="/wiki/Salmon_P._Chase" title="Salmon P. Chase">Salmon Chase</a> on March 4, 1873. In his second inaugural address, he reiterated the problems still facing the nation and focused on what he considered the chief issues of the day: Freedom and fairness for all Americans while emphasizing the benefits of citizenship for freed slaves. He also urged that the American Indian be "made a useful and productive member of society." Grant concluded his address with the words, "My efforts in the future will be directed towards the restoration of good feelings between the different sections of our common community".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016540_351-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016540-351">[334]</a></sup> In May of that year Wilson suffered a stroke; never fully recovering, he died in office on November 22, 1875.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016545Diller19961545_352-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016545Diller19961545-352">[335]</a></sup> With Wilson's loss, Grant relied on Fish's guidance more than ever.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981385_353-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981385-353">[336]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Panic_of_1873_and_loss_of_Congress">Panic of 1873 and loss of Congress</span></h3>
<p>As his first term was ending, Grant continued to work for a strong dollar, signing into law the <a href="/wiki/Coinage_Act_of_1873" title="Coinage Act of 1873">Coinage Act of 1873</a>, which effectively ended the legal basis for <a href="/wiki/Bimetallism" title="Bimetallism">bimetallism</a> (the use of both silver and gold as money), and established the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a> in practice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVenable201166.E2.80.9368_354-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVenable201166.E2.80.9368-354">[337]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-356" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-356">[r]</a></sup> The Coinage Act discontinued the <a href="/wiki/Seated_Liberty_dollar" title="Seated Liberty dollar">standard silver dollar</a> and established the <a href="/wiki/Gold_dollar" title="Gold dollar">gold dollar</a> as the sole monetary standard. The result was deflation, with lower prices. Silverites who wanted more money in circulation to raise prices farmers received denounced the move as the "Crime of 1873", claiming the deflation made mortgages more burdensome for farmers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinstein1967307.E2.80.93326_357-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinstein1967307.E2.80.93326-357">[339]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_Inflation_Bill_Veto.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A cartoon depicting Grant after vetoing the Inflation bill." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Grant_Inflation_Bill_Veto.jpg/220px-Grant_Inflation_Bill_Veto.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Grant_Inflation_Bill_Veto.jpg/330px-Grant_Inflation_Bill_Veto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Grant_Inflation_Bill_Veto.jpg/440px-Grant_Inflation_Bill_Veto.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="688" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_Inflation_Bill_Veto.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Grant is congratulated for vetoing the "inflation bill" in 1874.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Grant's second term saw renewed economic turmoil. In September 1873, <a href="/wiki/Jay_Cooke_%26_Company" title="Jay Cooke &amp; Company">Jay Cooke &amp; Company</a>, a New York brokerage house, collapsed after it failed to sell all of the bonds issued by <a href="/wiki/Jay_Cooke" title="Jay Cooke">Cooke's</a> <a href="/wiki/Northern_Pacific_Railway" title="Northern Pacific Railway">Northern Pacific Railway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a517_358-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a517-358">[340]</a></sup> The collapse sent ripples through Wall Street, and other banks and brokerages that owned railroad stocks and bonds were also ruined.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a517_358-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a517-358">[340]</a></sup> On September 20, the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Stock_Exchange" title="New York Stock Exchange">New York Stock Exchange</a> suspended trading for ten days.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981393_359-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981393-359">[341]</a></sup> Grant, who knew little about finance, traveled to New York to consult leading businessmen and bankers for advice on how to resolve the crisis, which became known as the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1873" title="Panic of 1873">Panic of 1873</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001576.E2.80.93579_360-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001576.E2.80.93579-360">[342]</a></sup> Grant believed that, as with the collapse of the Gold Ring in 1869, the panic was merely an economic fluctuation that affected bankers and brokers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a518_361-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a518-361">[343]</a></sup> He instructed the Treasury to buy $10 million in government bonds, injecting cash into the system. The purchases curbed the panic on Wall Street, but an industrial depression, later called the <a href="/wiki/Long_Depression" title="Long Depression">Long Depression</a>, nonetheless swept the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001576.E2.80.93579_360-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001576.E2.80.93579-360">[342]</a></sup> Many of the nation's railroads—89 out of 364—went bankrupt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981391Smith2001375.E2.80.93377_362-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981391Smith2001375.E2.80.93377-362">[344]</a></sup></p>
<p>Congress hoped inflation would stimulate the economy and passed what became known as the Inflation Bill in 1874. Many farmers and workingmen favored the bill, which would have added $64 million in greenbacks to circulation, but some Eastern bankers opposed it because it would have weakened the dollar.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981395_363-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981395-363">[345]</a></sup> Belknap, Williams, and Interior Secretary <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Delano" title="Columbus Delano">Columbus Delano</a><sup id="cite_ref-365" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-365">[s]</a></sup> told Grant a veto would hurt Republicans in the November elections. Grant believed the bill would destroy the credit of the nation, and he vetoed it despite their objections. Grant's veto, supported by Fish, placed him in the conservative faction of the Republican party, and was the beginning of the party's commitment to a strong gold-backed dollar.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001580.E2.80.93581_366-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001580.E2.80.93581-366">[347]</a></sup> Grant later pressured Congress for a bill to further strengthen the dollar by gradually reducing the number of greenbacks in circulation. When the Democrats gained a majority in the House of 169 to 109, after the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections,_1874" title="United States House of Representatives elections, 1874">1874 elections</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lame_duck_(politics)" title="Lame duck (politics)">lame-duck</a> Republican Congress did so before the Democrats took office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016550_367-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016550-367">[348]</a></sup> On January 14, 1875, Grant signed the <a href="/wiki/Specie_Payment_Resumption_Act" title="Specie Payment Resumption Act">Specie Payment Resumption Act</a> into law, which required gradual reduction of the number of greenbacks allowed to circulate and declared that specie payment (i.e., in gold or silver) would resume in 1879.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001580.E2.80.93581_366-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001580.E2.80.93581-366">[347]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gilded_Age_corruption_and_reform">Gilded Age corruption and reform</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_presidential_administration_scandals" title="Ulysses S. Grant presidential administration scandals">Ulysses S. Grant presidential administration scandals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_presidential_administration_reforms" title="Ulysses S. Grant presidential administration reforms">Ulysses S. Grant presidential administration reforms</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cover_of_Harper%27s_Weekly_Dec_7,_1872.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/50/Cover_of_Harper%27s_Weekly_Dec_7%2C_1872.jpg/220px-Cover_of_Harper%27s_Weekly_Dec_7%2C_1872.jpg" width="220" height="338" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/50/Cover_of_Harper%27s_Weekly_Dec_7%2C_1872.jpg/330px-Cover_of_Harper%27s_Weekly_Dec_7%2C_1872.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/50/Cover_of_Harper%27s_Weekly_Dec_7%2C_1872.jpg/440px-Cover_of_Harper%27s_Weekly_Dec_7%2C_1872.jpg 2x" data-file-width="748" data-file-height="1148" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cover_of_Harper%27s_Weekly_Dec_7,_1872.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cartoonist <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Nast" title="Thomas Nast">Thomas Nast</a> praises Grant for rejecting demands by Pennsylvania politicians to suspend civil service rules.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Grant served as president during the <a href="/wiki/Gilded_Age" title="Gilded Age">Gilded Age</a>, a time when the economy was open to speculation and unchecked western railroad expansion, which in turn fueled corruption in federal offices.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodward1957.27.27passim.27.27_368-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodward1957.27.27passim.27.27-368">[349]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016541_369-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016541-369">[350]</a></sup> Grant responded to charges of financial misconduct in all federal departments more than any 19th Century president.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974133.E2.80.93134_370-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974133.E2.80.93134-370">[351]</a></sup> Grant's trusting nature often mislead him to stubbornly remain loyal to his corrupt cabinet members or appointees.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001592McFeely1981407.E2.80.93415White2016538.E2.80.93539_371-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001592McFeely1981407.E2.80.93415White2016538.E2.80.93539-371">[352]</a></sup> Grant often saw their prosecutions as unjust and shielded them from attack even at the cost of his own reputation, unless evidence of personal misconduct was overwhelming.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001587_372-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001587-372">[353]</a></sup> No person linked any of the scandals together, except possibly Grant's personal secretary, <a href="/wiki/Orville_E._Babcock" title="Orville E. Babcock">Orville E. Babcock</a>, who indirectly controlled many cabinet departments and delayed federal investigations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974133.E2.80.93134_370-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974133.E2.80.93134-370">[351]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodward1957.27.27passim.27.27_368-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodward1957.27.27passim.27.27-368">[349]</a></sup></p>
<p>From 1869 to 1871, Grant's authorized the Army to raid and destroy illegal distilleries in New York.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalzell2014_373-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalzell2014-373">[354]</a></sup> In November 1871, <a href="/wiki/Collector_of_the_Port_of_New_York" title="Collector of the Port of New York">New York Collector</a> and a member of New York Senator <a href="/wiki/Roscoe_Conkling" title="Roscoe Conkling">Roscoe Conkling</a>'s political machine, was forced to resign.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974145_374-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974145-374">[355]</a></sup> Murphy, a Grant appointee, had become embroiled in a dispute with another faction of the Republican party over the jobs at his disposal and was investigated by Congress for mismanagement and corruption in office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974145_374-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974145-374">[355]</a></sup> In December, Grant appointed <a href="/wiki/Chester_A._Arthur" title="Chester A. Arthur">Chester A. Arthur</a>, another Conkling man, to replace Murphy, and administration of the Customs House steadily improved.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974145_374-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974145-374">[355]</a></sup> On March 3, 1873, Grant signed into law a congressional and presidential salary increase. The unpopular <a href="/wiki/Salary_Grab_Act" title="Salary Grab Act">Salary Grab Act</a> was repealed in December, although Grant was allowed to keep his pay raise.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001552.E2.80.93553_251-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001552.E2.80.93553-251">[242]</a></sup></p>
<p>The scandals escalated during Grant's second term, even reaching into Grant's inner circle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016554_375-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016554-375">[356]</a></sup> In 1874, a congressional investigation exposed corruption in the Treasury Department, known as the <a href="/wiki/Sanborn_incident" title="Sanborn incident">Sanborn incident</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974147.E2.80.93148_376-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974147.E2.80.93148-376">[357]</a></sup> Previously, in August 1872, acting Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/William_Adams_Richardson" title="William Adams Richardson">William Richardson</a> under Secretary Boutwell, hired <a href="/wiki/John_B._Sanborn" title="John B. Sanborn">John B. Sanborn</a> as an independent tax collector on a 50 percent commission basis, known as a <i>moiety</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001578McFeely1974147_377-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001578McFeely1974147-377">[358]</a></sup> Richardson later became Secretary of Treasury in March 1873.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974147_378-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974147-378">[359]</a></sup> Sanborn was given virtual control of the Treasury, and he used excessive intrusive methods of obtaining taxes from delinquent taxpayers and railroads, while Treasury agents were pressured to give him their own tax collection delinquency accounts to ensure Sanborn a maximum commission.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001578_379-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001578-379">[360]</a></sup> Congress condemned Richardson for allowing Sanborn to abuse the law but did not attempt Richardson's impeachment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974147.E2.80.93148_376-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974147.E2.80.93148-376">[357]</a></sup> When a motion of censure of Richardson was put on the House floor, Richardson resigned and Grant appointed him judge of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Court_of_Claims" title="United States Court of Claims">Court of Claims</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974147.E2.80.93148_376-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974147.E2.80.93148-376">[357]</a></sup> In June 1874, Grant signed into law the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Moiety_Acts" title="Anti-Moiety Acts">Anti-Moiety Act</a>, abolishing that system.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974133.E2.80.93134_370-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974133.E2.80.93134-370">[351]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Whiskeyring.jpg" class="image"><img alt="&quot;Uncle Sam&quot; cartoon tapping a Louisville whiskey barrel, captioned &quot;probe away&quot;" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Whiskeyring.jpg/250px-Whiskeyring.jpg" width="250" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Whiskeyring.jpg/375px-Whiskeyring.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Whiskeyring.jpg/500px-Whiskeyring.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="619" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Whiskeyring.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Harper%27s_Weekly" title="Harper's Weekly">Harper's Weekly</a></i> cartoon on Bristow's Whiskey Ring investigation</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Grant replaced Richardson as Treasury Secretary with <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_H._Bristow" class="mw-redirect" title="Benjamin H. Bristow">Benjamin H. Bristow</a>, a man known for his honesty,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974148_380-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974148-380">[361]</a></sup> who began a series of reforms in the department, including tightening up the detective force.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a556.E2.80.93557Kohn2000417Nevins192956_381-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a556.E2.80.93557Kohn2000417Nevins192956-381">[362]</a></sup> Discovering that millions of gallons of whiskey escaped taxation,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a556.E2.80.93557_382-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a556.E2.80.93557-382">[363]</a></sup> and having Grant's endorsement to act ("Let no guilty man escape"), Bristow in May 1875 struck down the <a href="/wiki/Whiskey_Ring" title="Whiskey Ring">Whiskey Ring</a>, seized 32 installations, arrested some 350 men, obtained 176 indictments that led to 110 convictions, and restored $3,150,000 to the Treasury.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974156Nevins192956Smith2001584Kohn2000417_383-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974156Nevins192956Smith2001584Kohn2000417-383">[364]</a></sup> When Bristow's investigation implicated Babcock as part of the Whiskey Ring, Grant became defensive, believing Babcock was the victim of a witch hunt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001591_384-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001591-384">[365]</a></sup> While denying immunity to minor Whiskey Ring conspirators, Grant worked to protect Babcock.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKohn2000417_385-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKohn2000417-385">[366]</a></sup> In 1876, a jury acquitted Babcock at a trial influenced by Grant's deposition in Babcock's favor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001592.E2.80.93593_386-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001592.E2.80.93593-386">[367]</a></sup> After the trial, Grant dismissed Babcock from the White House.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001591.E2.80.93593_387-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001591.E2.80.93593-387">[368]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-389" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-389">[t]</a></sup> Grant freed some Ring members after a few months in prison, and freed Ring founder John McDonald after 17 months in jail.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKohn2000417_385-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKohn2000417-385">[366]</a></sup></p>
<p>Grant's <a href="/wiki/United_States_Civil_Service_Commission" title="United States Civil Service Commission">Civil Service Commission</a> reforms had limited success, as some members of his cabinet implemented a merit system that increased the number of qualified candidates and relied less on Congressional patronage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001589.E2.80.93590Simon2002250_390-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001589.E2.80.93590Simon2002250-390">[370]</a></sup> Delano, however, exempted his department from competitive examinations, and Congress refused to enact permanent Civil Service reform.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001589_391-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001589-391">[371]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zachariah_Chandler" title="Zachariah Chandler">Zachariah Chandler</a>, who succeeded Delano in 1875, reformed the Interior Department and fired corrupt clerks in the Bureau of Indian Affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon2002250Patrick1968172_392-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon2002250Patrick1968172-392">[372]</a></sup> The same year, Grant fired Attorney General <a href="/wiki/George_Henry_Williams" title="George Henry Williams">George Henry Williams</a> after his wife was discovered to be involved in a bribery scandal, and appointed reformer <a href="/wiki/Edwards_Pierrepont" title="Edwards Pierrepont">Edwards Pierrepont</a> in his place.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016557_393-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016557-393">[373]</a></sup> Grant suggested other reforms as well, including a proposal that states should offer free <a href="/wiki/State_school" title="State school">public schooling</a> to all children; he also endorsed the <a href="/wiki/Blaine_Amendment" title="Blaine Amendment">Blaine Amendment</a>, which would have forbidden government aid to schools with religious affiliations.</p>
<p>When the Democrats took control of the House in December 1875, they launched a series of investigations seeking corruption in federal departments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981429_394-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981429-394">[374]</a></sup> The most damaging of which involved Secretary of War <a href="/wiki/William_W._Belknap" title="William W. Belknap">William W. Belknap</a> taking quarterly <a href="/wiki/Kickbacks" class="mw-redirect" title="Kickbacks">kickbacks</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Trader_post_scandal" title="Trader post scandal">Fort Sill tradership</a>, which led to his sudden resignation in February 1876.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a560.E2.80.93561Donovan2008104Simon2002252_395-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a560.E2.80.93561Donovan2008104Simon2002252-395">[375]</a></sup> Belknap was tried by the Senate that summer, but was not convicted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon2002252_396-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon2002252-396">[376]</a></sup> Congress also investigated and reprimanded Navy Secretary Robeson in July 1876 for bribery.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974153_397-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1974153-397">[377]</a></sup> In November 1876, Grant apologized to the nation and admitted mistakes in his administration, saying, "[f]ailures have been errors of judgement, not of intent."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981441.E2.80.93442_398-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981441.E2.80.93442-398">[378]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1876">Election of 1876</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1876" title="United States presidential election, 1876">United States presidential election, 1876</a></div>
<p>Even as Grant drew cheers at the opening of the <a href="/wiki/Centennial_Exposition" title="Centennial Exposition">Centennial Exposition</a> in May 1876, the collected scandals of his presidency, the country's weak economy, and the Democratic gains in the House led many in the Republican party to repudiate him in June.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981440.E2.80.93441Patrick1968255_399-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981440.E2.80.93441Patrick1968255-399">[379]</a></sup> Bristow was among the leading candidates to replace him, suggesting that a large faction desired an end to "<a href="/wiki/Grantism" title="Grantism">Grantism</a>" and feared that Grant would run for a third term.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981440.E2.80.93441_400-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981440.E2.80.93441-400">[380]</a></sup> Ultimately, Grant declined to run, but Bristow also failed to capture the nomination, as the convention settled on Governor <a href="/wiki/Rutherford_B._Hayes" title="Rutherford B. Hayes">Rutherford B. Hayes</a> of Ohio, a reformer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001586.2C_596_401-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001586.2C_596-401">[381]</a></sup> The Democrats nominated Governor <a href="/wiki/Samuel_J._Tilden" title="Samuel J. Tilden">Samuel J. Tilden</a> of New York.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001597_402-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001597-402">[382]</a></sup> Voting irregularities in three Southern states caused the election that year to remain undecided for several months.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001598_403-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001598-403">[383]</a></sup> Grant received a report that Democrats in the South were threatening violence and if Hayes won, Grant would be assassinated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016578.E2.80.93579_404-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016578.E2.80.93579-404">[384]</a></sup> Grant told Congress to settle the matter through legislation and assured both sides that he would not use the army to force a result, except to curb violence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001601.E2.80.93603_405-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001601.E2.80.93603-405">[385]</a></sup> On January 29, 1877, Grant signed legislation passed by Congress to form an <a href="/wiki/Electoral_Commission_(United_States)" title="Electoral Commission (United States)">Electoral Commission</a> to decide the matter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001602_406-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001602-406">[386]</a></sup> The Commission ruled that the disputed votes belonged to Hayes; to forestall Democratic protests, Republicans agreed to the <a href="/wiki/Compromise_of_1877" title="Compromise of 1877">Compromise of 1877</a>, in which the last troops were withdrawn from Southern capitals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001604_407-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001604-407">[387]</a></sup> The Republicans had won, but Reconstruction was over.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001604_407-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001604-407">[387]</a></sup> According to biographer Jean Edward Smith, "Grant's calm visage in the White House reassured the nation."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001603_408-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001603-408">[388]</a></sup></p>
