<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-presidency">Post-presidency</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Post-presidential_life_of_Ulysses_S._Grant" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-presidential life of Ulysses S. Grant">Post-presidential life of Ulysses S. Grant</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_tour_and_diplomacy">World tour and diplomacy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_%26_Bismarck_Meeting.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Grant_%26_Bismarck_Meeting.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Grant_%26_Bismarck_Meeting.tif.jpg" width="220" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Grant_%26_Bismarck_Meeting.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Grant_%26_Bismarck_Meeting.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Grant_%26_Bismarck_Meeting.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Grant_%26_Bismarck_Meeting.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="508" data-file-height="646" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_%26_Bismarck_Meeting.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Grant and Bismarck in 1878</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After leaving the White House, Grant and his family stayed with Fish in New York for two months before setting out on a world tour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981448.E2.80.93449_409-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981448.E2.80.93449-409">[389]</a></sup> Preparing for the tour, they arrived in Philadelphia on May 10, 1877, and were honored with celebrations during the week before their departure.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung1879a4_410-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung1879a4-410">[390]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016577_411-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016577-411">[391]</a></sup> On May 16, Grant and Julia left for England aboard the <a href="/wiki/SS_Indiana_(1873)" title="SS Indiana (1873)">SS <i>Indiana</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016590_412-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016590-412">[392]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung1879a5_413-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung1879a5-413">[393]</a></sup> The Hayes administration, aware of Grant's popularity in Europe, encouraged him to extend his tour and voyage around the world to strengthen American interests abroad, an unprecedented undertaking for a former President.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2016xi.E2.80.93xii.2C_2.E2.80.933_414-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2016xi.E2.80.93xii.2C_2.E2.80.933-414">[394]</a></sup> The first stop of the journey was in <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> in May, where enormous crowds greeted the ex-president and his entourage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981454.E2.80.93455_415-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981454.E2.80.93455-415">[395]</a></sup> Grant was lauded as the "Hero of Appomattox" and the Union general who defeated the Confederacy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001608_416-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001608-416">[396]</a></sup> The Grants dined with <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> at <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Castle" title="Windsor Castle">Windsor Castle</a>, and Grant gave several speeches in London.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a581.E2.80.93583_417-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a581.E2.80.93583-417">[397]</a></sup></p>
<p>After a tour on the continent, the Grants visited <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> and then spent time with their daughter Nellie in <a href="/wiki/Southhampton" class="mw-redirect" title="Southhampton">Southhampton</a>, who during Grant's presidency had married an Englishman, Algernon Sartoris, in a White House ceremony.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016547.E2.80.93548_418-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016547.E2.80.93548-418">[398]</a></sup> Grant and Julia journeyed to <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> and visited with <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Gambetta" title="Léon Gambetta">Léon Gambetta</a> whom Grant considered one of the greatest people he met on the tour. Next they departed for Italy, visiting <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Vesuvius" title="Mount Vesuvius">Mount Vesuvius</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981464_419-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981464-419">[399]</a></sup> The Grants spent Christmas 1877 aboard <a href="/wiki/USS_Vandalia_(1876)" title="USS Vandalia (1876)">USS <i>Vandalia</i></a>, a warship docked in <a href="/wiki/Palermo,_Italy" class="mw-redirect" title="Palermo, Italy">Palermo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981460.E2.80.93465_420-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981460.E2.80.93465-420">[400]</a></sup> After cruising the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a>, the Grants took a winter sojourn through Egypt and the Holy Land. They were in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> on March 3, 1878 and at the time of the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_San_Stefano" title="Treaty of San Stefano">Treaty of San Stefano</a>. Next, followed a visit to Greece, before they met with <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_XIII" title="Pope Leo XIII">Pope Leo XIII</a> at the Vatican.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981466.E2.80.93467_421-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981466.E2.80.93467-421">[401]</a></sup> In 1878, they visited the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> before moving on to Germany, where Grant discussed military matters with Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Otto von Bismarck</a>, telling him that in the final stages of the Civil War, the Union Army fought to preserve the nation and to "destroy slavery".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a585.E2.80.93586_422-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a585.E2.80.93586-422">[402]</a></sup> In Russia, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_II_of_Russia" title="Alexander II of Russia">Czar Alexander II</a> asked Grant about the future of the Plains Indians.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a608_423-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a608-423">[403]</a></sup> After touring <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, the Grants returned to England, via Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016589_424-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016589-424">[404]</a></sup> From England, the Grants left by private ship, sailing into the Mediterranean and stopping at <a href="/wiki/Marseilles" class="mw-redirect" title="Marseilles">Marseilles</a>, then across to <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> visiting Egypt hurriedly and on through the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> to India.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981471.E2.80.93473_425-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981471.E2.80.93473-425">[405]</a></sup></p>
<p>Grant initially viewed British colonization of India as "purely selfish", yet upon observation, he acknowledged that the colonial subjects were allowed to prosper.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981473.E2.80.93474_426-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981473.E2.80.93474-426">[406]</a></sup> After India, they toured <a href="/wiki/Burma" class="mw-redirect" title="Burma">Burma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siam" class="mw-redirect" title="Siam">Siam</a> (where Grant met with King <a href="/wiki/Chulalongkorn" title="Chulalongkorn">Chulalongkorn</a>), <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, and <a href="/wiki/French_Cochinchina" title="French Cochinchina">Cochinchina</a> (Vietnam).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a590.E2.80.93591_427-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a590.E2.80.93591-427">[407]</a></sup> Leaving Hong Kong, the Grants visited Canton, Shanghai, and Peking, China, where he criticized the autocratic attitude of Westerners toward the Chinese, which he compared to that of antebellum slaveowners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001612_428-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001612-428">[408]</a></sup> He declined to ask for an interview with the <a href="/wiki/Guangxu_Emperor" title="Guangxu Emperor">Guangxu Emperor</a>, a child of seven, but did speak with the head of government, <a href="/wiki/Prince_Gong_(Qing_dynasty)" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Gong (Qing dynasty)">Prince Gong</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Li_Hongzhang" title="Li Hongzhang">Li Hongzhang</a>, a leading general.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a591.E2.80.93592_429-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a591.E2.80.93592-429">[409]</a></sup> They discussed China's dispute with <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a>, and Grant agreed to serve as a mediator.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a593.E2.80.93594_430-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a593.E2.80.93594-430">[410]</a></sup> After crossing over to Japan on the <a href="/wiki/USS_Richmond_(1860)" title="USS Richmond (1860)"><i>USS Richmond</i></a> and meeting the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Meiji" title="Emperor Meiji">Emperor Meiji</a>, Grant, keeping his word, tried to convince Japan to make peace with the Chinese; Japan having the superior military, annexed the disputed islands a few weeks after Grant left the country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001612n.E2.80.93613n_431-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001612n.E2.80.93613n-431">[411]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016611.E2.80.93612_432-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016611.E2.80.93612-432">[412]</a></sup></p>
<p>Homesick, the Grants left Japan sailing on the <i><a href="/wiki/SS_City_of_Tokio" title="SS City of Tokio">City of Tokio</a></i> escorted by a Japanese man-of-war, crossed the Pacific and landed in San Francisco in September 1879, greeted by cheering crowds.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001613_433-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001613-433">[413]</a></sup> After a visit to <a href="/wiki/Yosemite_National_Park" title="Yosemite National Park">Yosemite Valley</a>, they returned at last to Philadelphia on December 16, 1879.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981477_434-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981477-434">[414]</a></sup> Their two-year and seven-month voyage around the world captured the popular imagination, and Republicans—especially those of the <a href="/wiki/Stalwart_(politics)" title="Stalwart (politics)">Stalwart</a> faction excluded from the Hayes administration—saw Grant in a new light.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001614.E2.80.93615_435-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001614.E2.80.93615-435">[415]</a></sup> The Republican nomination for 1880 was wide open after Hayes forswore a second term and many Republicans thought that Grant was the man for the job.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001614.E2.80.93615_435-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001614.E2.80.93615-435">[415]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_term_attempt">Third term attempt</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulysses_S._Grant_Trapeze_Cartoon_Keppler_Puck_1880.tif" class="image"><img alt="Grant, shown in a cartoon as an acrobat hanging from rings, holding up multiple politician/acrobats" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Ulysses_S._Grant_Trapeze_Cartoon_Keppler_Puck_1880.tif/lossy-page1-180px-Ulysses_S._Grant_Trapeze_Cartoon_Keppler_Puck_1880.tif.jpg" width="180" height="277" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Ulysses_S._Grant_Trapeze_Cartoon_Keppler_Puck_1880.tif/lossy-page1-270px-Ulysses_S._Grant_Trapeze_Cartoon_Keppler_Puck_1880.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Ulysses_S._Grant_Trapeze_Cartoon_Keppler_Puck_1880.tif/lossy-page1-360px-Ulysses_S._Grant_Trapeze_Cartoon_Keppler_Puck_1880.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="765" data-file-height="1177" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulysses_S._Grant_Trapeze_Cartoon_Keppler_Puck_1880.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In the Democratic magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Puck_(magazine)" title="Puck (magazine)">Puck</a></i>, cartoonist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Ferdinand_Keppler" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph Ferdinand Keppler">Joseph Ferdinand Keppler</a> lampooned Grant's alleged support of profiteering "rings" among his associates. (1880)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1880_Republican_National_Convention" title="1880 Republican National Convention">1880 Republican National Convention</a></div>
<p>Stalwarts, led by Grant's old political ally, <a href="/wiki/Roscoe_Conkling" title="Roscoe Conkling">Roscoe Conkling</a>, saw the ex-president's renewed popularity as a way for their faction to regain power. Opponents denounced the idea as a violation of the two-term rule that had been the norm since George Washington. Grant said nothing publicly, but he wanted the job and encouraged his men.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHesseltine1957432.E2.80.93439_436-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHesseltine1957432.E2.80.93439-436">[416]</a></sup> Elihu B. Washburne urged him to run; Grant demurred, saying he would be happy for the Republicans to win with another candidate, though he preferred <a href="/wiki/James_G._Blaine" title="James G. Blaine">James G. Blaine</a> to <a href="/wiki/John_Sherman" title="John Sherman">John Sherman</a>. Even so, Conkling and <a href="/wiki/John_A._Logan" title="John A. Logan">John A. Logan</a> began to organize delegates in Grant's favor. When <a href="/wiki/1880_Republican_National_Convention" title="1880 Republican National Convention">the convention</a> convened in Chicago in June, there were more delegates pledged to Grant than to any other candidate, but he was still short of a majority vote to capture the nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a600.E2.80.93601_437-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a600.E2.80.93601-437">[417]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the convention, Conkling nominated Grant with an elegant speech, the most famous line being: "When asked which state he hails from, our sole reply shall be, he hails from Appomattox and its famous apple tree."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a600.E2.80.93601_437-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a600.E2.80.93601-437">[417]</a></sup> With 370 votes needed for nomination, the first ballot had Grant at 304, Blaine at 284, Sherman at 93, and the rest scattered to minor candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981479.E2.80.93481_438-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981479.E2.80.93481-438">[418]</a></sup> Subsequent ballots followed, with roughly the same result; neither Grant nor Blaine could win. After thirty-six ballots, Blaine's delegates deserted him and combined with those of other candidates to nominate a compromise candidate: Representative <a href="/wiki/James_A._Garfield" title="James A. Garfield">James A. Garfield</a> of Ohio.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a602_439-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a602-439">[419]</a></sup> The 306 votes Grant received on the last ballot was not enough to secure the nomination. A procedural motion made the vote unanimous for Garfield, who accepted the nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001617_440-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001617-440">[420]</a></sup></p>
<p>Grant gave speeches for Garfield but declined to criticize the Democratic nominee, <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott_Hancock" title="Winfield Scott Hancock">Winfield Scott Hancock</a>, a general who had served under him in the Army of the Potomac.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a604.E2.80.93605_441-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a604.E2.80.93605-441">[421]</a></sup> Garfield won the popular vote by a narrow margin, but solidly won the Electoral College—214 to 155. After the election, Grant gave Garfield his public support, and pushed him to include Stalwarts in his administration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a607.E2.80.93609_442-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a607.E2.80.93609-442">[422]</a></sup> On July 2, 1881, Garfield was shot by an assassin and died on September 19. On learning of Garfield's death from a reporter, Grant wept bitterly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a613.E2.80.93614_443-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a613.E2.80.93614-443">[423]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_ventures">Business ventures</span></h3>
<p>When Grant had returned to America from his costly world tour, he had depleted most of his savings and needed to earn money and find a new home.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a611_444-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a611-444">[424]</a></sup> Wealthy friends bought him a home on Manhattan's <a href="/wiki/Upper_East_Side" title="Upper East Side">Upper East Side</a>, and to make an income, Grant, Jay Gould, and former Mexican Finance Secretary <a href="/wiki/Mat%C3%ADas_Romero" title="Matías Romero">Matías Romero</a> chartered the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Southern_Railroad" title="Mexican Southern Railroad">Mexican Southern Railroad</a>, with plans to build a railroad from Oaxaca to Mexico City.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981486.E2.80.93489_445-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981486.E2.80.93489-445">[425]</a></sup> Grant urged <a href="/wiki/Chester_A._Arthur" title="Chester A. Arthur">Chester A. Arthur</a>, who had succeeded Garfield as president in 1881, to negotiate a free trade treaty with Mexico. Arthur and the Mexican government agreed, but the United States Senate rejected the treaty in 1883.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981486.E2.80.93489_445-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981486.E2.80.93489-445">[425]</a></sup> The railroad was similarly unsuccessful, falling into bankruptcy the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981486.E2.80.93489_445-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981486.E2.80.93489-445">[425]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the same time, Grant's son Ulysses Jr. had opened a Wall Street brokerage house with <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Ward" title="Ferdinand Ward">Ferdinand Ward</a>. Ward was regarded as a rising star and the firm, Grant &amp; Ward, was initially successful.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981488.E2.80.93491_446-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981488.E2.80.93491-446">[426]</a></sup> In 1883, Grant joined the firm and invested $100,000 of his own money.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a619_447-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a619-447">[427]</a></sup> To encourage investment, Ward paid investors abnormally high interest, by pledging the company's <a href="/wiki/Security_(finance)" title="Security (finance)">securities</a> on multiple loans in a process called <i><a href="/wiki/Rehypothecation" class="mw-redirect" title="Rehypothecation">rehypothecation</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981488.E2.80.93491_446-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981488.E2.80.93491-446">[426]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016269_448-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016269-448">[428]</a></sup> Ward, in collusion with banker James D. Fish, kept secret from <a href="/wiki/Bank_examiner" title="Bank examiner">bank examiners</a>, retrieved the firm's securities from the company's bank vault.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016627.E2.80.93629_449-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016627.E2.80.93629-449">[429]</a></sup> When the trades went bad, multiple loans came due, all backed up by the same collateral. Historians agree that Grant was likely unaware of Ward's intentions, but it is unclear how much Buck Grant knew. In May 1884, enough investments went bad to convince Ward that the firm would soon be bankrupt. Ward told Grant of the impending failure, but assured Grant that this was a temporary shortfall.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a620.E2.80.93621_450-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a620.E2.80.93621-450">[430]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016627.E2.80.93629_449-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016627.E2.80.93629-449">[429]</a></sup> Grant approached businessman <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Vanderbilt" title="William Henry Vanderbilt">William Henry Vanderbilt</a>, who gave him a personal loan of $150,000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981492.E2.80.93493_451-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981492.E2.80.93493-451">[431]</a></sup> Grant invested the money in the firm, but it was not enough to save it from failure. Essentially penniless, but compelled by a sense of personal honor, he repaid what he could with his Civil War mementos and the sale or transfer of all other assets.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerry2004xxix_452-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerry2004xxix-452">[432]</a></sup> Vanderbilt took title to Grant's home, although he allowed the Grants to continue to reside there, and pledged to donate the souvenirs to the federal government and insisted the debt had been paid in full.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016632.E2.80.93633Brands2012a620.E2.80.93621_453-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016632.E2.80.93633Brands2012a620.E2.80.93621-453">[433]</a></sup> Grant was distraught over Ward's deception and asked privately how he could ever "trust any human being again."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001621_454-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001621-454">[434]</a></sup> In March 1885, as his health was failing, Grant testified against both Ward and Fish.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBadeau1887447_455-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBadeau1887447-455">[435]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMackowskiWhite2015169_456-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMackowskiWhite2015169-456">[436]</a></sup> Ward was convicted of <a href="/wiki/Grand_larceny" class="mw-redirect" title="Grand larceny">fraud</a> in October 1885, months after Grant's death, and served six and a half years in prison.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a620.E2.80.93621_450-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a620.E2.80.93621-450">[430]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Memoirs.2C_pension.2C_and_death">Memoirs, pension, and death</span></h3>
<div class="mw-stack mobile-float-reset" style="box-sizing: border-box; float:right;">
<div style="overflow: hidden; margin: 1px;">
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Grant_in_1885.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Grant sitting in a porch chair wrapped in blankets" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/US_Grant_in_1885.jpg/220px-US_Grant_in_1885.jpg" width="220" height="259" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/US_Grant_in_1885.jpg/330px-US_Grant_in_1885.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/US_Grant_in_1885.jpg/440px-US_Grant_in_1885.jpg 2x" data-file-width="884" data-file-height="1040" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Grant_in_1885.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Grant working on his memoirs in June 1885, less than a month before his death</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_Funeral_Train3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Drawing of steam engine and train approaching station with an honor guard at attention" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Grant_Funeral_Train3.jpg/220px-Grant_Funeral_Train3.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Grant_Funeral_Train3.jpg/330px-Grant_Funeral_Train3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Grant_Funeral_Train3.jpg/440px-Grant_Funeral_Train3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2680" data-file-height="2000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_Funeral_Train3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Grant's funeral train at West Point</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>To restore his family's income and reputation, Grant wrote several articles on his Civil War campaigns for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Century_Magazine" title="The Century Magazine">The Century Magazine</a></i> at $500 each. The articles were well received by critics, and the editor, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Underwood_Johnson" title="Robert Underwood Johnson">Robert Underwood Johnson</a>, suggested that Grant write a book of memoirs, as Sherman and others had done. Grant's articles would serve as the basis for several chapters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981_457-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981-457">[437]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the summer of 1884, Grant complained of a sore throat but put off seeing a doctor until late October when he learned it was cancer, possibly caused by his frequent cigar smoking.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016636Waugh2009277_458-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016636Waugh2009277-458">[438]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-460" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-460">[u]</a></sup> Grant chose not to reveal the seriousness of his condition to his wife, who soon found out from Grant's doctor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016637_461-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016637-461">[440]</a></sup> Before being diagnosed, Grant was invited to a Methodist service for Civil War veterans in <a href="/wiki/Ocean_Grove,_New_Jersey" title="Ocean Grove, New Jersey">Ocean Grove, New Jersey</a>, on August 4, 1884, receiving a standing ovation from more than ten thousand veterans and others; it would be his last public appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981495.E2.80.93496_462-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981495.E2.80.93496-462">[441]</a></sup> In March of the following year, the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Times">New York Times</a> announced that Grant was dying of cancer and a nationwide public concern for the former president began.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a622.E2.80.93626_463-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a622.E2.80.93626-463">[442]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001625_464-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001625-464">[443]</a></sup> Knowing of Grant and Julia's financial difficulties, Congress sought to honor him and restored him to the rank of General of the Army with full retirement pay (Grant's assumption of the Presidency in 1869 had required that he resign his commission and forfeit his pension).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001625_464-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001625-464">[443]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016641_465-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016641-465">[444]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite his debilitating illness, Grant worked diligently on his memoirs at his home in New York City, and then from <a href="/wiki/Grant_Cottage_State_Historic_Site" title="Grant Cottage State Historic Site">a cottage</a> on the slopes of <a href="/wiki/Mount_McGregor_(mountain)" title="Mount McGregor (mountain)">Mount McGregor</a>, finishing only days before he died.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a629.E2.80.93630_466-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a629.E2.80.93630-466">[445]</a></sup> Grant's memoirs treat his early life and time in the Mexican–American War briefly and are inclusive of his life up to the end of the Civil War.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001627_467-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001627-467">[446]</a></sup> <i>Century</i> magazine offered Grant a book contract with a 10 percent royalty, but Grant accepted a better offer from his friend, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain">Mark Twain</a>, who proposed a 75 percent royalty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981501.E2.80.93505_468-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981501.E2.80.93505-468">[447]</a></sup> The book, <i><a href="/wiki/Personal_Memoirs_of_Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Personal Memoirs of Ulysses S. Grant">Personal Memoirs of Ulysses S. Grant</a></i>, was a critical and commercial success. In the end, Julia Grant received about $450,000 in royalties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981501.E2.80.93505_468-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981501.E2.80.93505-468">[447]</a></sup> The memoir has been highly regarded by the public, military historians, and literary critics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson1962131.E2.80.93173_469-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson1962131.E2.80.93173-469">[448]</a></sup> Grant portrayed himself in the persona of the honorable Western hero, whose strength lies in his honesty and straightforwardness. He candidly depicted his battles against both the Confederates and internal army foes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell1990189.E2.80.93209_470-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell1990189.E2.80.93209-470">[449]</a></sup> Twain called the <i>Memoirs</i> a "literary masterpiece." Given over a century of favorable literary analysis, reviewer <a href="/wiki/Mark_Perry_(author)" title="Mark Perry (author)">Mark Perry</a> states that the <i>Memoirs</i> are "the most significant work" of American <a href="/wiki/Non-fiction" title="Non-fiction">non-fiction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerry2004234.E2.80.93235_471-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerry2004234.E2.80.93235-471">[450]</a></sup> Grant's successful autobiography pioneered a method for ex-presidents and veterans to earn money.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZimmerman2010_472-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZimmerman2010-472">[451]</a></sup></p>
<p>After a year-long struggle with the cancer, surrounded by his family, Grant died at 8 o'clock in the morning in the Mount McGregor cottage on July 23, 1885, at the age of 63.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981517_473-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981517-473">[452]</a></sup> Sheridan, then Commanding General of the Army, ordered a day-long tribute to Grant on all military posts, and President <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a> ordered a thirty-day nationwide period of mourning. After private services, the honor guard placed Grant's body on a special <a href="/wiki/Funeral_train" title="Funeral train">funeral train</a>, which traveled to <a href="/wiki/West_Point,_New_York" title="West Point, New York">West Point</a> and New York City. A quarter of a million people viewed it in the two days before the funeral. Tens of thousands of men, many of them veterans from the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Army_of_the_Republic" title="Grand Army of the Republic">Grand Army of the Republic</a> marched with Grant's casket drawn by two dozen horses to <a href="/wiki/Riverside_Park_(Manhattan)" title="Riverside Park (Manhattan)">Riverside Park</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Morningside_Heights,_Manhattan" title="Morningside Heights, Manhattan">Morningside Heights</a> neighborhood of <a href="/wiki/Upper_Manhattan" title="Upper Manhattan">Upper Manhattan</a>. His pallbearers included Union generals Sherman and Sheridan, Confederate generals Simon Bolivar Buckner and Joseph E. Johnston, Admiral David Dixon Porter, and Senator John A. Logan, the head of the GAR.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a633.E2.80.93635_474-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a633.E2.80.93635-474">[453]</a></sup> Following the casket in the seven-mile-long procession were President Cleveland, the two living former presidents Hayes and Arthur, all of the President's Cabinet, as well as the justices of the Supreme Court.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200119_475-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200119-475">[454]</a></sup></p>
<p>Grant's body was laid to rest in Riverside Park, first in a temporary tomb, and then—twelve years later, on April 17, 1897—in the <a href="/wiki/Grant%27s_Tomb" title="Grant's Tomb">General Grant National Memorial</a>, also known as "Grant's Tomb", the largest <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum" title="Mausoleum">mausoleum</a> in North America. Attendance at the New York funeral topped 1.5 million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a633.E2.80.93635_474-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a633.E2.80.93635-474">[453]</a></sup> Ceremonies were held in other major cities around the country, while Grant was eulogized in the press and likened to <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> and Abraham Lincoln.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaugh2009215.E2.80.93259_476-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaugh2009215.E2.80.93259-476">[455]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_reputation">Historical reputation</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulysses_S._Grant_from_West_Point_to_Appomattox.jpg" class="image"><img alt=" Colored print of portrait of Grant surrounded by scenes from his military career" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Ulysses_S._Grant_from_West_Point_to_Appomattox.jpg/280px-Ulysses_S._Grant_from_West_Point_to_Appomattox.jpg" width="280" height="369" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Ulysses_S._Grant_from_West_Point_to_Appomattox.jpg/420px-Ulysses_S._Grant_from_West_Point_to_Appomattox.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Ulysses_S._Grant_from_West_Point_to_Appomattox.jpg/560px-Ulysses_S._Grant_from_West_Point_to_Appomattox.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5626" data-file-height="7412" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulysses_S._Grant_from_West_Point_to_Appomattox.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Clockwise from lower left: Graduated at West Point 1843; Chapultepec 1847; Drilling his Volunteers 1861; Fort Donelson 1862; Shiloh 1862; Vicksburg 1863; Chattanooga 1863; Commander-in-Chief 1864; Lee's Surrender 1865</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Very few presidential reputations have shifted as dramatically as Grant's.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner2012_477-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner2012-477">[456]</a></sup> Hailed across the North as the winning general in a great war, his nomination as president seemed inevitable. Grant's popularity declined with congressional investigations into corruption in his administration and Custer's defeat at the Battle of the Little Big Horn. In 1877, there was bipartisan approval of Grant's peaceful handling of the electoral crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBadeau1887250_478-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBadeau1887250-478">[457]</a></sup> Grant's reputation soared during his well-publicized world tour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012b45_479-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012b45-479">[458]</a></sup></p>
<p>At his death, Grant was seen as "a symbol of the American national identity and memory", when millions turned out for his funeral procession in 1885 and attended the 1897 dedication of his tomb.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaugh20092_480-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaugh20092-480">[459]</a></sup> Grant's popularity increased in the years immediately after his death. At the same time, commentators and scholars portrayed his administration as the most corrupt in American history. As the popularity of the pro-Confederate <a href="/wiki/Lost_Cause" class="mw-redirect" title="Lost Cause">Lost Cause</a> movement increased early in the 20th century, a more negative view became increasingly common.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981521.E2.80.93522_481-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981521.E2.80.93522-481">[460]</a></sup> As they had early in the Civil War, Grant's new critics charged that he was a reckless drunk, and in light of his presidency, that he was also corrupt. In the 1930s, biographer <a href="/wiki/William_B._Hesseltine" title="William B. Hesseltine">William B. Hesseltine</a> noted that Grant's reputation deteriorated because his enemies were better writers than his friends.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200114_482-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200114-482">[461]</a></sup> In 1931, Frederic Paxson and Christian Bach in the <i><a href="/wiki/Dictionary_of_American_Biography" title="Dictionary of American Biography">Dictionary of American Biography</a></i> praised Grant's military vision and his execution of that vision in defeating the Confederacy, but of his political career, the authors were less complimentary. Speaking specifically of the scandals, they wrote that "personal scandal has not touched Grant in any plausible form, but it struck so close to him and so frequently as to necessitate the vindication of his honor by admitting his bad taste in the choice of associates."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaxsonBach1931500_483-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaxsonBach1931500-483">[462]</a></sup></p>
<p>Views of Grant reached new lows as he was seen as an unsuccessful president and an unskilled, if lucky, general.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012b45_479-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012b45-479">[458]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Catton" title="Bruce Catton">Bruce Catton</a> and <a href="/wiki/T._Harry_Williams" title="T. Harry Williams">T. Harry Williams</a> began the reassessment of Grant's military career in the 1960s, shifting the analysis of Grant as victor by brute force to that of successful and skillful strategist and commander.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERafuse2007851_484-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERafuse2007851-484">[463]</a></sup> Even for scholars with a particular concern for the plight of former slaves and Indians, Grant left a problematic legacy and, with changing attitudes toward warfare after the end of the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>, Grant's military reputation suffered again.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERafuse2007855.2C_871.E2.80.93874_485-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERafuse2007855.2C_871.E2.80.93874-485">[464]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_S._McFeely" title="William S. McFeely">William S. McFeely</a> won the <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize_for_Biography_or_Autobiography" title="Pulitzer Prize for Biography or Autobiography">Pulitzer Prize</a> for his critical 1981 biography that emphasized the failure of Grant's presidency to carry out lasting progress and concluded that "he did not rise above limited talents or inspire others to do so in ways that make his administration a credit to American politics."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981522_486-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981522-486">[465]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Y._Simon" title="John Y. Simon">John Y. Simon</a> in 1982 responded to McFeely: "Grant's failure as President&#160;... lies in the failure of the Indian peace policy and the collapse of Reconstruction&#160;... But if Grant tried and failed, who could have succeeded?"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon1982221_487-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon1982221-487">[466]</a></sup> Simon praised Grant's first term in office, arguing that it should be "remembered for his staunch enforcement of the rights of freedmen combined with conciliation of former Confederates, for reform in Indian policy and civil service, for successful negotiation of the <i>Alabama</i> Claims, and for delivery of peace and prosperity." According to Simon, the Liberal Republican revolt, the Panic of 1873, and the North's conservative retreat from Reconstruction weakened Grant's second term in office, although his foreign policy remained steady.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon2002253_488-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon2002253-488">[467]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USA_grants_tomb.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Neoclassical structure with dome" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/USA_grants_tomb.jpg/220px-USA_grants_tomb.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/USA_grants_tomb.jpg/330px-USA_grants_tomb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/USA_grants_tomb.jpg/440px-USA_grants_tomb.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1494" data-file-height="1992" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USA_grants_tomb.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Grant%27s_Tomb" title="Grant's Tomb">General Grant National Memorial</a>, known colloquially as "Grant's Tomb", is the largest <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum" title="Mausoleum">mausoleum</a> in North America, and one of the largest in the world.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Historians' views have grown more favorable since the 1990s, appreciating Grant's protection of African Americans and his peace policy towards Indians, even where those policies failed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaugh20092_480-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaugh20092-480">[459]</a></sup> Grant's reputation rose further with Jean Edward Smith's 2001 biography. Smith argued that the same qualities that made Grant a success as a general carried over to his political life to make him, if not a successful president, then certainly an admirable one.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeigley20011105_489-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeigley20011105-489">[468]</a></sup> Smith wrote that "the common thread is strength of character—an indomitable will that never flagged in the face of adversity&#160;... Sometimes he blundered badly; often he oversimplified; yet he saw his goals clearly and moved toward them relentlessly."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200115_490-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200115-490">[469]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Brooks_Simpson" class="mw-redirect" title="Brooks Simpson">Brooks Simpson</a> continued the trend in the first of two volumes on Grant in 2000, although the work was far from a hagiography.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERafuse2007862.E2.80.93863_491-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERafuse2007862.E2.80.93863-491">[470]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/H._W._Brands" title="H. W. Brands">H. W. Brands</a>, in his more uniformly positive 2012 book, wrote favorably of Grant's military and political careers alike, saying:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>As commanding general in the Civil War, he had defeated secession and destroyed slavery, secession's cause. As President during Reconstruction he had guided the South back into the Union. By the end of his public life the Union was more secure than at any previous time in the history of the nation. And no one had done more to produce the result than he.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a636_492-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a636-492">[471]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>As Reconstruction scholar <a href="/wiki/Eric_Foner" title="Eric Foner">Eric Foner</a> wrote, Brands gave "a sympathetic account of Grant's forceful and temporarily successful effort as president to crush the Ku Klux Klan, which had inaugurated a reign of terror against the former slaves."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner2012_477-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner2012-477">[456]</a></sup> Foner criticized Grant for not sending military aid to Mississippi during the 1875 election to protect African Americans from threats of violence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner2012_477-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner2012-477">[456]</a></sup> According to Foner, "Grant's unwillingness to act reflected the broader Northern retreat from Reconstruction and its ideal of racial equality."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner2012_477-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner2012-477">[456]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-494" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-494">[v]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to historian Brooks Simpson, Grant was on "the right side of history". Simpson said, "[w]e now view Reconstruction ... as something that should have succeeded in securing equality for African-Americans, and we see Grant as supportive of that effort and doing as much as any person could do to try to secure that within realm of political reality." <a href="/wiki/John_F._Marszalek" title="John F. Marszalek">John F. Marszalek</a> said, "You have to go almost to Lyndon Johnson to find a president who tried to do as much to ensure black people found freedom."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBauman2015_495-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBauman2015-495">[473]</a></sup> In 2016, <a href="/wiki/Ronald_C._White" title="Ronald C. White">Ronald C. White</a> continued this trend with a biography that historian <a href="/wiki/T._J._Stiles" title="T. J. Stiles">T. J. Stiles</a> said, "solidifies the positive image amassed in recent decades, blotting out the caricature of a military butcher and political incompetent engraved in <a href="/wiki/National_memory" title="National memory">national memory</a> by <a href="/wiki/Jim_Crow" class="mw-redirect" title="Jim Crow">Jim Crow</a> era historians."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStiles2016_496-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStiles2016-496">[474]</a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout the 20th century, historians ranked his presidency near the bottom. In the 21st century, his military reputation is strong, while most scholars <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">rank his presidency</a> well below average.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner2012_477-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner2012-477">[456]</a></sup> His accomplishments as President have been overlooked due to corruption charges of his Cabinet members and appointees during his administration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner2012Lasner2002_497-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner2012Lasner2002-497">[475]</a></sup> Concerning his post-presidential trip around the world, historian Edwina S. Campbell said that Grant "invented key aspects of the foreign-policy role of the modern American presidency, and created an image abroad of the United States that endures to this day."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell20161_498-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell20161-498">[476]</a></sup> White viewed Grant as "an exceptional person and leader" and his presidency, although marred by corruption charges, "defended the political rights of African Americans, battled against the Ku Klux Klan and voter suppression, reimagined Indian policy, rethought the role of the federal government in a changing America, and foresaw that as the United States would now assume a larger place in world affairs, a durable peace with Great Britain would provide the nation with a major ally."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016xxiv.2C_xxvi_499-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016xxiv.2C_xxvi-499">[477]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Memorials_and_presidential_library">Memorials and presidential library</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_cultural_depictions" title="Ulysses S. Grant cultural depictions">Ulysses S. Grant cultural depictions</a></div>
<p>Several memorials honor Grant. In addition to his mausoleum – <a href="/wiki/Grant%27s_Tomb" title="Grant's Tomb">Grant's Tomb</a> in New York City – there is the <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_Memorial" title="Ulysses S. Grant Memorial">Ulysses S. Grant Memorial</a> at the foot of <a href="/wiki/Capitol_Hill" title="Capitol Hill">Capitol Hill</a> in Washington, D.C.<sup id="cite_ref-500" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-500">[478]</a></sup> Created by sculptor <a href="/wiki/Henry_Merwin_Shrady" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry Merwin Shrady">Henry Merwin Shrady</a> and architect <a href="/wiki/Edward_Pearce_Casey" title="Edward Pearce Casey">Edward Pearce Casey</a>, and dedicated in 1922, which overlooks the <a href="/wiki/Capitol_Reflecting_Pool" title="Capitol Reflecting Pool">Capitol Reflecting Pool</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ulysses_S._Grant_Memorial_501-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ulysses_S._Grant_Memorial-501">[479]</a></sup> In 2015, restoration work began, which is expected to be completed before the bicentennial of Grant's birth in 2022.<sup id="cite_ref-Architect_of_the_Capitol_502-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Architect_of_the_Capitol-502">[480]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_National_Historic_Site" title="Ulysses S. Grant National Historic Site">Ulysses S. Grant National Historic Site</a> near St. Louis, and several other sites in Ohio and Illinois memorialize Grant's life.<sup id="cite_ref-503" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-503">[481]</a></sup> There are smaller memorials in Chicago's <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Park" title="Lincoln Park">Lincoln Park</a> and Philadelphia's <a href="/wiki/Fairmount_Park" title="Fairmount Park">Fairmount Park</a>. Named in his honor are <a href="/wiki/Grant_Park_(Chicago)" title="Grant Park (Chicago)">Grant Park</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Grant_County,_Indiana" title="Grant County, Indiana">several counties in western and midwestern states</a>. On June 3, 1891, a bronze statue of Grant by Danish sculptor <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Gelert" title="Johannes Gelert">Johannes Gelert</a> was dedicated at Grant Park in Galena, Illinois.<sup id="cite_ref-504" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-504">[482]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-505" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-505">[483]</a></sup> From 1890 to 1940, part of what is now <a href="/wiki/Kings_Canyon_National_Park" title="Kings Canyon National Park">Kings Canyon National Park</a> was called <i>General Grant National Park</i>, named for the <a href="/wiki/General_Grant_tree" class="mw-redirect" title="General Grant tree">General Grant sequoia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-506" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-506">[484]</a></sup> Grant has appeared on the front of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_fifty-dollar_bill" title="United States fifty-dollar bill">United States fifty-dollar bill</a> since 1913. In 1921, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ulysses_S._Grant_Centenary_Association&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ulysses S. Grant Centenary Association (page does not exist)">Ulysses S. Grant Centenary Association</a> was founded with the goal of coordinating special observances and erecting monuments in recognition of Grant's historical role. The venture was financed by the minting of 10,000 gold dollars and 250,000 half dollars (depicted below). The coins were minted and issued in 1922, commemorating the 100th anniversary of Grant's birth.<sup id="cite_ref-507" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-507">[485]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-508" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-508">[486]</a></sup> Grant has also appeared on <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidents_on_U.S._postage_stamps#Ulysses_S._Grant" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. presidents on U.S. postage stamps">several U.S. postage stamps</a>, the first one issued in 1890, five years after his death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaimann2006_509-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaimann2006-509">[487]</a></sup></p>
<center>
<div class="thumb tmulti tnone center">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;max-width:802px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:266px;max-width:266px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:US-$50-GC-1928-Fr-2404.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Paper currency, double image of obverse (with Grants image) and reverse (with Capitol building image)" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/US-%2450-GC-1928-Fr-2404.jpg/264px-US-%2450-GC-1928-Fr-2404.jpg" width="264" height="231" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/US-%2450-GC-1928-Fr-2404.jpg/396px-US-%2450-GC-1928-Fr-2404.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/US-%2450-GC-1928-Fr-2404.jpg/528px-US-%2450-GC-1928-Fr-2404.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1773" data-file-height="1550" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Grant has appeared on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_fifty-dollar_bill" title="United States fifty-dollar bill">United States fifty-dollar bill</a> since 1913.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:348px;max-width:348px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulysses_S._Grant_One_Dollar_Gold_Piece,_1922_issue.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Image of Grant on the One dollar gold piece, depicting date of mintage, 1922" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Ulysses_S._Grant_One_Dollar_Gold_Piece%2C_1922_issue.JPG/346px-Ulysses_S._Grant_One_Dollar_Gold_Piece%2C_1922_issue.JPG" width="346" height="174" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Ulysses_S._Grant_One_Dollar_Gold_Piece%2C_1922_issue.JPG/519px-Ulysses_S._Grant_One_Dollar_Gold_Piece%2C_1922_issue.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Ulysses_S._Grant_One_Dollar_Gold_Piece%2C_1922_issue.JPG/692px-Ulysses_S._Grant_One_Dollar_Gold_Piece%2C_1922_issue.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1238" data-file-height="624" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Grant on the one dollar gold piece, issued 1922 on the 100th anniversary of birth. See also: <a href="/wiki/File:US_Grant_Half_Dollar_1922.jpg" title="File:US Grant Half Dollar 1922.jpg">Grant half dollar</a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_1890_2-5c.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Image of first Grant U.S. Postage stamp, issued in 1890, brown, five cents" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Grant_1890_2-5c.jpg/180px-Grant_1890_2-5c.jpg" width="180" height="205" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Grant_1890_2-5c.jpg/270px-Grant_1890_2-5c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Grant_1890_2-5c.jpg/360px-Grant_1890_2-5c.jpg 2x" data-file-width="550" data-file-height="625" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The first U.S. Postage stamp honoring Grant, issued 1890</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">
<center><big>Ulysses S. Grant honored on currency and postage</big></center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</center>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>In May 2012, the Ulysses S. Grant Foundation, on the institute's fiftieth anniversary, selected <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_State_University#Mitchell_Memorial_Library" title="Mississippi State University">Mississippi State University</a> as the permanent location for Ulysses S. Grant's <a href="/wiki/Presidential_library" title="Presidential library">presidential library</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-510" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-510">[488]</a></sup> Historian John Y. Simon edited Grant's letters into a 32-volume scholarly edition published by <a href="/wiki/Southern_Illinois_University_Press" title="Southern Illinois University Press">Southern Illinois University Press</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-511" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-511">[489]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>
<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Grant's step-grandmother Sarah Simpson, an educated woman who read French classical literature, spoke up for the name <a href="/wiki/Odysseus" title="Odysseus">Ulysses</a>, the legendary, ancient Greek hero.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite20169.E2.80.9310_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite20169.E2.80.9310-11">[11]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Biographer Edward G. Longacre attributes Grant's parents' decision to their recognition of his hatred of music.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to Grant, the S. did not stand for anything. Hamer had used it to stand for Simpson, Grant's mother's maiden name, or possibly for Grant's brother, whose name was Simpson.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198112Smith200124.2C_83_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198112Smith200124.2C_83-24">[22]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Subject to isolation and gloomy rainy weather, many of the officers took to drinking at Fort Humboldt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a71.E2.80.9373_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a71.E2.80.9373-68">[65]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-74"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-74">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Historical accounts vary somewhat. <a href="/wiki/William_McFeely" class="mw-redirect" title="William McFeely">William McFeely</a> said that Grant left the army simply because he was "profoundly depressed".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198155_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely198155-72">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jean_Edward_Smith" title="Jean Edward Smith">Jean Edward Smith</a> maintains Grant's resignation was too sudden to be a calculated decision.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200187_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200187-73">[69]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-103"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-103">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Frémont refused Lincoln's order to overturn his August 30 proclamation that emancipated Confederate slaves<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016168_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016168-102">[97]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-211"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-211">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Republicans kept the name "<a href="/wiki/National_Union_Party_(United_States)" title="National Union Party (United States)">Union</a>" on their ticket as had been done earlier at their 1864 convention.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[204]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-217"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-217">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Residents of Galena gave Grant the home in 1865 as thanks for his war service.<sup id="cite_ref-home_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-home-215">[208]</a></sup> After his presidential term ended in 1877, Grant visited the home occasionally. Maintenance of the home as a memorial to Grant started in 1904 and continues today.<sup id="cite_ref-nhl_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nhl-216">[209]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-236"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-236">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/John_Schofield" title="John Schofield">John Schofield</a>, who was Secretary of War under Johnson, was asked by Grant to remain in that position until he could appoint his own man in office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016473_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016473-235">[227]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-261"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-261">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1876, two decisions of the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Reese" title="United States v. Reese">United States v. Reese</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Cruikshank" title="United States v. Cruikshank">United States v. Cruikshank</a></i>) also did much to undermine Reconstruction-era laws that protected the rights of black Americans. Grant nominee <a href="/wiki/Morrison_Waite" title="Morrison Waite">Morrison Waite</a> wrote the court's opinions joined by others, while another Grant nominee <a href="/wiki/Ward_Hunt" title="Ward Hunt">Ward Hunt</a> dissented.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981387.E2.80.93389_238-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981387.E2.80.93389-238">[229]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-263"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-263">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Grant's religious faith also influenced his policy towards Indians, believing that the "Creator" did not place races of men on earth for the "stronger" to destroy the "weaker".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2016491_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2016491-262">[252]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-274"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-274">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bison were hunted to the point of extinction during the latter 1800s, Yellowstone National Park was the only remaining place in the country where free roaming herds persisted.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">[262]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-284"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-284">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Urged by his Secretary of War Rawlins, Grant initially supported recognition of Cuban belligerency, but Rawlins's death on September 6, 1869, removed any cabinet support for military intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon2002249_283-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon2002249-283">[271]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-290"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-290">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The international tribunal awarded the United States $15,500,000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2001512.E2.80.93515_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2001512.E2.80.93515-289">[276]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-294"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-294">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Báez government was under an insurgency led by <a href="/wiki/Gregorio_Luper%C3%B3n" title="Gregorio Luperón">Gregorio Luperón</a>. Báez had arrested an American, Davis Hatch, who had supported Luperon. Davis was held without trial and originally threatened with execution, compromising negotiations. (The sentence was later reduced to banishment from the island.)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981341-342_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981341-342-293">[279]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-315"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-315">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Under the gold standard, the value of currency (coins, fractional paper monies, and standard bank notes) was directly linked to the price of gold.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurdekinSiklos201324.E2.80.9325_314-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurdekinSiklos201324.E2.80.9325-314">[299]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-348"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-348">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Greeley died after election day but before the day the Electoral College voted, as a result, Greeley's running mate, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Gratz_Brown" title="Benjamin Gratz Brown">Benjamin Gratz Brown</a>, received most of the electoral votes Greeley would have had.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a499_347-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2012a499-347">[331]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-356"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-356">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The gold standard and deflation economy remained in effect into the mid-1890s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurdekinSiklos201325_355-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurdekinSiklos201325-355">[338]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-365"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-365">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Grant and Delano, his second Secretary of Interior, were third cousins.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorter2005185_364-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorter2005185-364">[346]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-389"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-389">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">McFeely, writing in 1981, believed that Grant knew of Babcock's guilt, while Smith, in 2001, believed the evidence against Babcock was circumstantial at best.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981415Smith2001591_388-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely1981415Smith2001591-388">[369]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-460"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-460">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Today, medical historians believed he suffered from a T<sub>1</sub>N<sub>1</sub> carcinoma of the tonsillar fossa.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERenehanLowry1995377.E2.80.93383_459-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERenehanLowry1995377.E2.80.93383-459">[439]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-494"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-494">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1883, the U.S. Supreme Court overruled the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1875" title="Civil Rights Act of 1875">Civil Rights Act of 1875</a> that guaranteed African Americans "full and equal enjoyment of the accommodations, advantages, facilities and privileges"; the ruling setting up a legal foundation for <a href="/wiki/Jim_Crow" class="mw-redirect" title="Jim Crow">Jim Crow</a> segregation throughout the South until the 1960's.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPusey2014_493-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPusey2014-493">[472]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
